4.3. Electrocatalysts for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells

4.3.1. Earth-abundant hydrogen and oxygen electrocatalysts. 
As illustrated in Fig. 17a, two types of fundamental reactions are involved in AEMWEs (HER and OER) and AEMFCs (HOR and ORR), respectively. Currently, there are many reports on AEMWECs/AEMFCs employing noble metals/noble metal oxides (e.g., Pt, Pd, Ir, IrO2) as hydrogen and oxygen electrocatalysts to achieve comparable a power density to that in PEMWEs/PEMFCs.197,198 They to some degree overlooked that one of the biggest benefits of using AEMWEs and AEMFCs is their capability to utilize non-noble-metal-based catalysts; therefore, the study of non-noble based electrocatalysts is rather significant and represents a great advance toward the practical deployment of these devices. Meanwhile, the costly noble metals and unsteady metal exports and supply would inevitably impede their long-term application.199,200 In principle, the development of advanced catalysts is highly dependent on the mechanical understanding of hydrogen and oxygen electrocatalysis chemistry.201–203 Unfortunately, an accurate understanding of the reaction kinetics of the HER/HOR and OER/ORR, together with the specific role of cations and ions in these reactions, poses a great challenge for researchers.204–208 To this end, we follow three steps to present a practical catalyst development concept, including a clear understanding of the electrochemical processes of the HER/HOR and OER/ORR, the establishment of a universal activity descriptor, and the construction of catalyst discovery scheme, as depicted in Fig. 17b. Given that the HER and HOR are reversible processes in hydrogen electrocatalysis, the HER and HOR will be discussed together to give a clear understanding of the chemistry of hydrogen electrocatalysis as well as the activity descriptor in the HER/HOR. Meanwhile, an outline of oxygen electrocatalysis chemistry and the activity descriptor for the OER/ORR will also be given. In terms of the scaling relationship, Sabatier's principle suggests that an optimal catalyst should possess moderate, neither too strong nor too weak, binding energy toward reaction intermediates (e.g., atoms, molecules, etc.), and this correlation is normally plotted as a function of the Gibbs free energy that is usually obtained by means of theoretical calculations (Fig. 17c).209,210 To make it quantitative and effective to guide the catalyst design on the experimental scale, the scaling relationship can be further replotted as a function of bulk physiochemical features such as the degree of 3d filling, eg electrons, electronegativity, etc.211 Although the bulk feature cannot provide very precise relations with intermediates as the surface properties do during adsorption/desorption, it offers a chance to explore the underlying mechanisms, which can be examined by theoretical simulations. In this regard, finding the scientific principles of hydrogen and oxygen electrocatalysis, understanding the chemical reaction descriptor, and the screening of efficient catalysts are of pivotal importance. For the HER/HOR and OER/ORR, we focus more attention on non-noble-metal-based electrocatalysts composed of earth-abundant elements to summarize their theoretical and experimental advances, accompanied by many fascinating strategies, including defect engineering, single-atom confinement, heterostructure building, etc. (Fig. 17d).
 Fig. 17  (a) Polarization curves and potential range for hydrogen (HER/HOR) and oxygen (OER/ORR) electrocatalyses. (b) The development scheme for low-cost and high-performance electrocatalysts. (c) Scaling relationship translation for hydrogen and oxygen electrocatalytic reactions, from a linear relation to a volcano relation. (d) Electrocatalyst engineering strategies. 


4.3.2. Chemistry of hydrogen electrocatalysis and HER/HOR activity descriptor. 
HER/HOR processes are two-electron-coupled electrode reactions involved in water electrolysis and H2–O2 fuel cells. The alkaline HER/HOR mechanism is a combination of three elementary steps: the Volmer step, the Heyrovsky step, and/or the Tafel step (eqn (4.8), (4.9) and (4.10)). Volmer step: H2O + e−  H* + OH−(4.8) Heyrovsky step: H2O + e− + H*  H2 + OH−(4.9) Tafel step: H* + H*  H2,(4.10)where H* represents an adsorbed H atom (Had) on the surface active site, and the alkaline HER proceeds via the Volmer–Heyrovsky or Volmer–Tafel step, while the HOR follows the Tafel–Volmer or Heyrovsky–Volmer mechanism. The catalyst design approaches as well as the discovery of optimal catalysts are highly dependent on having an in-depth understanding of the fundamental HER/HOR process. Deciphering the scientific issues for the pH-dependent HER/HOR kinetics as well as finding a universal catalytic activity descriptor, therefore, is significant for the further development of high-performance electrocatalysts. Fig. 18 gives a clear overview of the key stages during the development of the HER/HOR activity descriptor. The pioneering studies simply concentrated on the HER branch, and the researchers extracted a linear relationship between the HER exchange current density on monometallic surfaces and the metal–hydrogen binding energy, thereby generating a volcano plot (Fig. 18a).212,213 The volcano plot suggests that the ideal HER catalysts should possess balanced hydrogen binding energies for the Had adsorption and desorption, which fits well with the Sabatier principle.214 Later, with the assistance of DFT calculations, Nørskov et al. obtained a similar volcano curve of the hydrogen chemisorption energies on metal surfaces and experimental exchange currents, which demonstrated the correlation between the hydrogen binding energy and the HER exchange current.215 Since then, the metal–hydrogen binding energy (HBE) is widely accepted as an HER activity descriptor for a variety of metals and alloys.215–217 When the electrolyte changed from acidic to alkaline, slower HER kinetics by 2–3 orders of magnitude was observed for a wide range of electrocatalysts (PGMs) irrespective of their HBEs, implying that the alkaline HER kinetics might be impacted by other parameters.218,219 In view of this, Yan et al. ascribed the decreased HER/HOR kinetics to the stronger hydrogen binding energy in an alkaline electrolyte, which is associated with the sluggish Volmer step in the HER/HOR.220–222 Notably, the HBE here proposed by Yan et al. is an experimental value, which correlates with the peak potential of the oxidation of underpotential-deposited hydrogen (HUPD) (Fig. 18b). The pH-dependent kinetics of Pt, Ir, and Pd electrodes are all linearly related to the position of the HUPD desorption peak, directly proving the correctness of the HBE concept. From the derived equation (eqn (4.11)): HBE or Emetal-H = –Epeak × F,(4.11)where F is the Faraday constant and Epeak is the peak potential of the oxidation of HUPD. The energy difference could vary by 12.5–13.5 kJ mol−1 from pH = 0 to pH = 13, and the acid/alkaline reaction rate ratio (γacid/γalkaline) is in the range of 120 to 200, which is in agreement with the experimentally observed reaction difference.223 Later, Yan et al. proposed a refined HBE theory and claimed that it is the apparent Gibbs free energy of hydrogen adsorption, ΔGH,app, rather than hydrogen adsorption ΔGH, that is correlated with the HUPD desorption peak.219 According to Yan et al. (Fig. 18c), ΔGH,app can be calculated using the equation ΔGH,app = ΔGH − ΔGH2O, where ΔGH and ΔGH2O are the intrinsic Gibbs free energy and the Gibbs free energy of water adsorption, respectively. In the refined HBE concept, changing the electrolyte (in going from acids to bases) simply weakens ΔGH2O, while ΔGH remains constant. Consequently, ΔGH,app increases with increasing electrolyte pH and matches the shifted HUPD desorption peak. On the other hand, recent molecular dynamics modeling on Pt(100) that was based on quantum mechanics also demonstrated the weakened water adsorption under high pH conditions, in agreement with the refined HBE.224 The inconsistency in the HBE or the refined HBE concept, however, is that the pH-dependent HBE trend is not observed for the Pt(111) electrode.225 In addition, the good HER activity but unsatisfactory HOR activity observed for MoS2 contradicts the idea that the HBE should control the HER and HOR in a similar way.228,229 These results manifest that the HBE or refined HBE might not be a universal descriptor. Later, it was found that the HOR/HER activity can be enhanced by decorating Pt(111) with oxophilic metals (e.g., Ni, Co, Ru, etc.).226,230,231 Accordingly, Markovic's group proposed that both Had and OHad species are reactant intermediates, which is also known as bifunctional theory (Fig. 18d). In terms of the HER, the incorporation of oxophilic species promotes water dissociation with the generation of Had, which accelerates the Volmer step and contributes to the enhanced overall reaction. With regards to the HOR, the oxophilic species promote the adsorption of reactive OHad, which is vital for the Heyrovsky or Volmer step. In general, the overall HER/HOR kinetics is rationalized by the balance between water dissociation, Had, and OHad adsorption. Considering that the multiple adsorption intermediates are greatly impacted by the catalyst composition and surface structure, the researchers constructed single-crystal electrodes with controlled Had binding strength and fine-tuned OHad binding strength, and further found that the HER activity is correlated with the hydrogen and hydroxide binding strength.232 Apart from the bifunctional theory, the potential of the zero (free) charge (pzfc) theory, the 2B (bifunctional and hard acid soft base (HASB)) concept, etc. have emerged as an HER/HOR activity descriptor (Fig. 18e).225,227,233–239 So far, the aforementioned assumptions do describe the HER/HOR activity somehow, but the underlying science and inconsistencies remain to be addressed in further investigations. More direct observations and dynamical monitoring of the multiple reactant species (e.g., OHad, H2O, AM+, etc.) during the reaction, and the determination of the explicit role of additives might benefit the construction of a universal descriptor in the future.
 Fig. 18  Overview of the development of hydrogen electrocatalysis descriptors. (a) Hydrogen binding energy theory proposed by Nørskov et al. Reproduced with permission from ref. 215 Copyright 2005 IOP Publishing. (b) Hydrogen binding energy theory derived from experimental results and proposed by Yan et al. Reproduced with permission from ref. 220 Copyright 2016 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (c) The refined hydrogen binding energy proposed by Yan et al. Reproduced with permission from ref. 219 Copyright 2018 IOP Publishing. (d) The bifunctional theory proposed by Markovic et al. Reproduced with permission from ref. 226 Copyright 2012 Springer Nature. (e) The newly developed pzfc and 2B proposals by Jia et al. Reproduced with permission from ref. 227 Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society. 


4.3.3. Chemistry of oxygen electrocatalysis and OER/ORR activity descriptor. 
The oxygen evolution and oxidation are two reversible reactions coupled with the four-proton-electron transfer processes that take place at the anode and cathode of AEMWEs and AEMFCs, respectively. The overall reaction equation of the OER/ORR under alkaline media can be written as (eqn (4.12)): OER/ORR: 4OH− ↔ 4e− + 2H2O + O2,(4.12)where the overall equation can be classified into four elementary steps (eqn (4.13), (4.14), (4.15) and (4.16)): OH− ↔ OH* + e−(4.13) OH* + OH− ↔ H2O + O* + e−(4.14) O* + OH− ↔ OOH* + e−(4.15) OOH* + OH− ↔ H2O + O2 + e−.(4.16)Here, the OH*, O*, and OOH* are reactant intermediates and * represents a surface-active site. From the viewpoint of transition state theory (Fig. 19a), the reaction starts from the initial state and then goes to the transition state before ending with a final state.240 During the transition state, electrochemical free energy is required to drive the reaction to pass through each reaction pathway. Here, the presence of a catalyst can significantly reduce the reaction energy barrier through the interaction between the reaction intermediates and the catalyst active sites. Unlike the HER/HOR reaction with only one intermediate (H*), the three intermediates (Fig. 19b) in the OER/ORR are strongly correlated, which makes it rather challenging to construct scaling relations. Furthermore, the very complicated surface compositions and chemical states of each category of electrocatalyst intensify the difficulty in building a universal OER/ORR activity descriptor. Benefiting from our comprehension of oxygen electrocatalysis chemistry and advanced characterization techniques, the OER/ORR activity scaling relations on metals, metal single atoms (graphene-based support), metal oxides, perovskite oxides, etc. have almost been established, which will be discussed in detail. For metals and metal alloys, Nørskov and co-authors employed DFT-based calculations and calculated the Gibbs free energy of two key intermediates (O and OH) on the surface of Pt(111).241,242 Furthermore, this model was further extended to calculate the binding energies of O*, OH*, and OOH* for a range of other metals, where a volcano correlation between the calculated binding energy of O* and the ORR reactivity was obtained (Fig. 19c). Moreover, the relationship between ΔGOOH* and ΔGOH* was depicted as ΔGOOH* = ΔGOH* + 3.2 ± 0.2 eV (Fig. 19d), which can be found to apply universally to both close-packed (111) and open-packed (100) facets of metals and their alloys. Metal single atoms have achieved significant success in the past several years in the OER/ORR field, owing to their high catalytic activity and economic viability for practical applications.243–245 Similar to single-atom catalysts (SACs) in the HER/HOR, the tremendous research on SACs for the OER/ORR has also stimulated researchers to explore the underlying structure–activity relationships. Based on this, Zeng's group summarized the behavior of many transition metal single atoms on graphene and derived a scaling relation of adsorption free energies ΔGOH*, as shown in Fig. 19e–f.246 After a critical analysis of the adsorption energy, the authors employed more realistic concepts (valence electrons in the occupied d orbital of the metal element (θd) and electronegativity (E)) to predict, screen, and guide the rational design of new catalysts.246
 Fig. 19  (a) Transition state theory for illustrating the distinctive role of a catalyst in decreasing reaction barriers for a catalytic reaction. Reproduced with permission from ref. 240 Copyright 2015 RSC Publishing. (b) Gibbs free energy diagram for the oxygen evolution and reduction reactions. (c) ORR volcano plot for metals. (d) ORR theoretical limiting potential plot for face-centred cubic (fcc) (111) and (100) facets of metals and alloys. Reproduced with permission from ref. 242 Copyright 2017 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (e) Scatter plot of the adsorption free energy of OH versus the descriptor φ for all single transition metal (TM) atoms supported on macrocyclic molecules. (f) Scatter plot of adsorption free energy of H versus the descriptor φ for all single TM atoms supported on macrocyclic molecules. Reproduced with permission from ref. 246 Copyright 2018 Springer Nature. 


4.3.4. Recent progress on the earth-abundant hydrogen and oxygen electrocatalysts. 
Although the alkaline HER/HOR activity descriptor is debatable, a consensus has been reached that the interfacial water molecules and Had/OHad play an unavoidable role in the HER/HOR processes.225,227,229,239,247–249 Also, this is true for studying the OER/ORR activity descriptors. To this end, a series of typical metals, metal single atoms, metal compounds (e.g., oxides, dichalcogenides, phosphides, etc.), metal alloys, metal-based heterostructures, etc. will be thoroughly covered and compared. On the one hand, the explicit role of small molecules, ions, and species (e.g., Had, OHad) in the HER/HOR and OER/ORR, as well as a better understanding of the related innovative mechanisms will be elucidated. On the other hand, some excellent catalyst design strategies and a range of easily scaled catalyst manufacturing methods for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells will be discussed.
4.3.4.1. Single-atom catalysts (SACs) for HER/HOR and OER/ORR. 
Single-atom catalysts (SACs) have achieved significant progress in heterogeneous catalysis, such as CO oxidation, the water–gas shift reaction, electrocatalytic reactions, etc., owing to their efficiency in full atom utilization and excellent activity.243,250–253 As shown in Fig. 20a, the three important compositions in the SACs are the metal central ions, the surrounding ligands, and the host materials.250,254,255 By changing the central metal atoms, the coordination environmental, and/or the host materials, the catalyst properties (e.g., activity, selectivity, and stability) can be modulated accordingly. A typical example is that researchers mainly concentrated on oxide hosts for the earlier SACs (e.g., FeOx, CeO2, or TiO2) because of their strong chemical stability in thermal catalytic reactions (e.g., CO oxidation).256,257 Later, carbon-based materials were widely selected as hosts, stemming from their high electronic conductivity, good resistance to acids/bases, and abundant coordination environment.258–260 Since then, carbon-based SACs have been widely reported with excellent activities for HER, HOR, OER, ORR, etc.250,261Fig. 20b gives some good examples of SACs for HER from the C3–Ni bonding to Ni–C6 configuration,262,263 and then to Ni–N3–O2 coordination,264,265 following the tuning concept of coordination chemistry. On the other hand, non-carbon based supports (e.g., metal carbides, metal oxides, metal hydroxides, etc.) also emerged to build SACs to boost electrochemical performances.266–277 The metal atoms in these compounds might induce structural distortions and charge redistributions, which are expected to bring vacancies, phase transformations, or strains that trigger a considerable improvement in catalytic properties. Ru-doped Ni5P4 (Ni5P4–Ru) was verified with the formation of Ru–P/Ru–Ni bonds (Fig. 20b), and electrons were transferred from Ni/P to Ru, leading to enhanced HER activity because water adsorption is more favorable on Ni5P4–Ru (Ru top sites) than on bare Ni5P4 (Ni top sites).268 Introducing Co and Ni atoms into MoSe2 would substitute them for Mo atoms in the basal plane and give rise to the formation of Co–MoSe2 and Ni–MoSe2.278 Interestingly, compared with Ni–MoSe2, Co–MoSe2 possesses similar adsorption energy towards H2O and Had due to the minor difference in ΔGH2O and ΔGH, but much reduced HER kinetics, in turn demonstrating that OHad adsorption and OH− desorption are enhanced on Ni–MoSe2 and are critical to the alkaline HER process. Modulations of the local atomic configuration, defect engineering, support effects, etc. are commonly employed approaches that have been demonstrated to be effective.279–281 It is worth noting that these typical SACs reported with decent HER activity have not been actively studied in the HOR, and/or their HOR activity is unsatisfactory, even though HER/HOR is a reversible reaction, which poses a question that the activity descriptor of the HER/HOR for SACs has not been identified. To this end, Li's group employed atomic Ni to manipulate the electronic structure of Ru nanocrystals and discussed its influence on HOR activity.274 In RuNi1 nanocrystals (NCs), the Ru–Ni bonding in RuNi1 suggests that the electronic structure of Ru is well-tuned with the incorporation of atomically dispersed Ni. As expected, the HOR mass activity of RuNi1 (2.7 A mg−1) at 50 mV overpotential is much higher than those of the Ru–Ni bimetallic alloy (0.427 A mg−1) and Pt/C (0.426 A mg−1) (Fig. 20c). In Fig. 20d, DFT-based calculations demonstrate that the incorporation of atomic Ni could significantly reduce the hydrogen adsorption energy, particularly when compared with the pure Ru catalyst. Moreover, the energy barrier of the Volmer rate-determining step (rds) was decreased from 1.067 to 0.966 eV, which also boosted the fast HOR kinetics. These studies suggest the inevitable role of Had and OHad in the alkaline HER/HOR, although typical single atom configurations with HER and HOR that can be both carefully and comparatively investigated to examine the intermediates are very few. Pacchioni and co-authors proposed a new model to explain the activity descriptor in the HER.282 Based on the assumption that SACs can be considered as surface analogues of organometallic complexes, Nørskov's theory here is not well suited to the intermediate model of SACs, where the dihydrogen and/or dihydride complexes are easily formed. In contrast, the adsorptions of both the first H atom (ΔG0H(1)) and the second H atom (ΔG0H(2)) need to be considered because HMH dihydride complexes (M is a metal) are usually formed and observed on a series of single atoms including Pt atoms on N–Gr (where a carbon atom of the graphene (Gr) lattice is replaced by a N atom), MoS2, defect-rich graphene, etc.283 Consequently, the HER kinetics of SACs is highly associated with ΔG0H(1) and ΔG0H(2), implying that moderate adsorption energies towards H1 and H2 need to be satisfied simultaneously to attain an ideal HER activity, as shown in Fig. 20e. The exchange current as a function of ΔG0H(1) and ΔG0H(2) gives rise to the formation of a three–dimensional volcano plot in Fig. 20f. Taking metal single atoms anchored on N-doped graphene (M@N-Gr) as an example, their HER kinetics predicted by MH intermediate theory (Nørskov's theory, Fig. 20g, I) and the two-intermediate model (Fig. 20g, II) is illustrated. Here, i0/imax is defined as the descriptor, where i0 denotes the exchange current and imax is the maximum exchange current. Apparently, according to the two-intermediate model, V, Cr, Co, Ni, Cu, and Pd do not exhibit good HER activity as they did in the MH intermediate theory. In conclusion, this work reconsiders the rationale of Nørskov's theory in the determination of the HER activity. The pH-dependent kinetics of SACs and the rationality of this concept in the prediction of HER/HOR activity remain to be answered in future studies. SACs have also achieved tremendous progress in oxygen electrocatalysis owing to their many fascinating features, among which, Fe–N4 has been demonstrated to be the most promising PGM-free catalyst to substitute for Pt or Pt-based alloys, given its relatively high activity and four-electron selectivity towards the ORR.284–287 The current reported ORR activity of Fe/N/C catalysts (0.02 A cm−2@0.9 ViR-free), however, is far behind the 2025 target (0.44 A cm−2@0.9 ViR-free) for fuel cell technologies set by U.S. Department of Energy (DOE) to achieve the economic viability of fuel cells in light-duty vehicles.286 Based on the previous studies, the critical challenges for SACs in the ORR lie in their low electrochemical site density (ECSD) and inferior durability, which result in unsatisfactory power/energy density and very limited lifespan.288–290 Given that the dynamical reaction process and very complicated electron-transfer steps involve oxygen-related species, improving our mechanical understanding of oxygen electrocatalytic chemistry and designing high-performance ORR catalysts are still major tasks in the next stage. As mentioned in Fig. 20a, the ORR activity of SACs is mainly determined by the metal center, host, and ligand. Aside from the judicious choice of metal centers, ligand manipulations of SACs were widely reported. Most of the earlier reported metal–N4 single atom configuration relates to metal atoms that are bonded by four pyridinic N atoms,259,291,292 while Wu et al. demonstrated that
pyrrolic-type Fe–N4 coordination can also boost the ORR activity and achieve good fuel cell performance.293 Following this concept, Chen and co-authors designed nitrogen and phosphorus dual-coordinated iron active sites (Fe–N3P) and confirmed that more efficient O2 molecule adsorption and *O2 dissociation to *OOH can be achieved in contrast to the normal Fe–N4 model (Fig. 21a), thus significantly improving their catalytic performance in zinc-air batteries compared to the Pt/C benchmark.294 Meanwhile, Ma's group employed the concept of “Janus materials” and synthesized a dual single atomic site composed of Ni–N4 and Fe–N4 on the inner and outer walls of graphene hollow nanospheres (GHSs).295 Wang and co-workers reported single-site Ru cation coordination to manipulate the local ligand environments and the electronic structure via fabricating an Ru/LiCoO2 SAC with atomically dispersed Ru–Co pair sites (Fig. 21a).296 Many other interesting works in terms of metal center tuning and ligand tuning in SACs have been published.284,297–301 In this regard, an atomic-level understanding of the catalytic mechanism of the ORR might provide some new thoughts. As described in Fig. 21b, Liu and co-authors studied the mechanism of Fe–N4 from the viewpoint of electron spin.302 Specifically, the authors synthesized Fe single atom catalysts and identified that the Fe–N4 moieties can be classified into Fe–N4C12 (D1), Fe–N4C10 (D2), and N–Fe–N4C10 (D3), respectively, by using 57Fe Mössbauer spectra. Based on the values of the isomer shift (IS) and quadrupole splitting (QS), the spin state of Fe2+ in D1, D2, and D3 were assigned to low, intermediate, and high spin, respectively. The high correlation between the ORR mass activities of single-Fe-atom catalysts and the D3 moiety content strongly suggests that N–Fe–N4C10 is the critical component for high ORR activity. In the initial stage (polarized at 0.9 V), the operando57Fe Mössbauer spectra and Raman spectra confirmed that the D3 content was decreased with an increase in the relative D1 content, indicating O2 adsorption on D3 sites and the generation of O2−–FeN5 intermediate. During this process, the spin state of Fe2+ in D3 evolves from high spin to low spin, accompanied by a decreased isomer shift (IS) from 0.77 to 0.39 mm s−1. When polarized at 0.7 V, the D3 content was increased with a reduction in the relative D1 content, manifesting O2 adsorption on D1 sites and the formation of O2−–FeIIN4 intermediate. Meanwhile, both QS and IS showed an increase from 0.80 to 2.46 mm s−1 and 0.37 to 0.77 mm s−1, respectively, suggesting that the Fe2+ spin state in D1 changed from low spin to high spin, with Fe2+ moving out of the N4 square plane. Polarization at a higher bias (0.5 V) resulted in a continuously increasing content of O2−–FeIIN4 intermediate, again proving the dynamic ORR activity of D1 at higher biasing. It has to be noted that the preferential adsorption of O2 molecules on D3 near the onset potential demonstrates that N–Fe–N4C10 is a more active site towards the ORR in alkaline media. A fully dynamic ORR cycle on N–Fe–N4C10 moieties is presented in Fig. 21b. The O2 molecule adsorption on N–Fe–N4C10 sites stimulates the movement of the central Fe atom partially and completely to the N4-plane with the formation of OH− and *O2 intermediates, respectively. The interaction between the Fe 3d orbital (dxz) and side-on coordinated O2 π* orbital promotes the d-orbital splitting and results in the conversion of high spin Fe2+ to low spin Fe2+. The DFT results further confirmed that the energy barrier of orbital interaction between D3 and O2 is remarkably lower than that in the case of D1 and D2. On the other hand, Yu and co-authors, for the first time, depicted the relationship between the single atomic inter-site distance (dsite) of Fe–N4 and its ORR activity.303 By carefully controlling the metal loading content, Fe SAs with inter-site distances ranging from ∼7.8 to ∼0.5 nm were generated. Fig. 21c gives four typical EXAFS spectra of Fe–N4 samples, and the dsite of each sample estimated from STEM images is presented. Given that surface interrogation scanning electrochemical microscopy (SI-SECM) has been proved to offer insight into the first O2 adsorption kinetics on transition metal sites, the rate constant (k′) for oxygen binding on Fe(II)–N4 as a function of dsite was calculated via SI-SECM and is shown in Fig. 21d. A sudden jump appeared at the dsite values between 1.2 and 0.7 nm in the plot, which indicates a remarkable improvement of the oxygen activation activity, which is also in agreement with the TOF values. Furthermore, the authors deepened the study and employed 57Fe Mössbauer spectra and found that the catalytic activity of Fe–N4 was correlated with FeN4C12 (D1) and FeN4C10 (D2). The ORR activity difference of dsite can be ascribed to the difference in the ratio of D1 to D2, originating from the Fe spin states. As the two Fe atoms become closer, the energy level of the Fe dz2 orbital is downshifted, resulting in a higher degree of filling of the partially occupied d orbitals and eventually leading to weakened bonding strength of the over-strongly adsorbed hydroxyl intermediates. The dsite-dependent ΔG*OH plot in Fig. 21e acquired from DFT calculations demonstrates this interpretation. Also, the derived volcano plot of ORR overpotentials against ΔG*OH provides a new avenue to design and prepare single atom catalysts with reasonable metal loading. In contrast to SACs in the ORR, the fundamental challenges of SACs in the OER mainly originate from the unstable structural stability and inferior activity induced by ultrahigh overpotential. Even though carbonaceous materials possess many competitive features in electrocatalysis, their susceptibility to corrosion in the OER potential range in alkaline media would intensify catalyst dissolution,304,305 which calls for the development of other support materials such as metal oxides, hydroxides, etc., to improve the overall structural stability of SACs. To this end, Thomas et al. employed transition metal carbides (TMCs) as supports to design SACs because of their very robust hardness, thermal stability, chemical corrosion resistance, and high electrical conductivity. Fig. 22a presents a schematic image of the WCx-FeNi catalyst with the Fe and Ni atoms immobilized on the WCx support. The HAADF-STEM image and the corresponding intensity profile along the (−12−1) plane (Fig. 22b and c) further validate the existence of Ni and Fe single atoms, which was demonstrated by the weak bonding (Ni–W and Fe–W). As expected, WCx-FeNi exhibited 237 mV overpotential towards the OER at 10 mA cm−2, and this value further decreased to 211 mV when the atomic loading content increased from 0.38 to 1.14 mg cm−2. The reaction pathways are illustrated in Fig. 22d, where the adsorption free energy values toward OH* and O* on WCx-FeNi and WCx-FeNi–OH (surface oxidized) are reduced. Apart from the support engineering, Lu and co-authors synthesized a Co1/TaS2 catalyst where Co SAs were confined in the TaS2 monolayer, and they discussed the OER activity from the viewpoint of electron spin-density.306 The intercalated Co SAs were found to occupy the high-spin (HS) sites (bonding at the hollow site, CoHS) and Ta sites (Co replacing Ta atoms, CoTa), although the CoHS sites were calculated to have smaller formation energy. The CoHS and CoTa sites correspond to the Co2+ and Co3+ species, and the Co1/TaS2 catalyst with tunable CoHS and CoTa sites was obtained by controlling the content of Co SAs. DFT calculations revealed that the CoHS sites have much reduced energy barrier than CoTa sites and the pristine TaS2 surface, so they serve as the real OER active centers. In Fig. 22e, the adjacent CoTa sites were validated to favor the formation of O*, while the neighboring CoHS sites result in a larger energy barrier from OOH* to O2. Furthermore, the CoHS–CoTa sites were calculated to have a medium magnetic moment (2.100 μB) for the CoHS atom, which consequently benefits the formation of CoHS–O single bonding and leads to optimal O* adsorption/desorption as well as excellent OER activity and durability. As shown in Fig. 22f, the correlation between the OER activity and the Co spin density value of the Co1/TaS2 catalyst as a function of the doped Co content is established. These studies of SACs anchored on non-carbon-based supports for the alkaline OER,307–309 open an avenue to diversify the single-atom catalyst design strategy and strengthen the activity and durability of such catalysts toward the alkaline OER via support engineering, electron spin state, etc., which also provides insights, knowledge, and principles for the followed catalyst design.310,311 Based on the aforementioned studies, Li et al. employed a series of metal single atoms (Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, Ag, and Mo) that immobilized on the carbon support and aimed to propose a universal thought in the determination of HER/HOR activity. It was found that changing the metal centers would result in a different water adsorption behavior as well as OH binding energy. Hence, the authors concluded that the hybridization between M 3d and O 2p orbitals might be a dominant reason for the varied species adsorption and HER/HOR activities. For Mn–N–C, Mn t52ge0g, with an unoccupied eg state, it is easier to possess strong overlaps with O 2p orbitals and hence higher adsorption capability. For Cu–N–C, Cu t62ge4g, the full eg orbitals of Cu lead to negligible H2O/OH adsorption (Fig. 22g). This thought is based on the analysis of crystal field theory and square pyramidal geometry for the configurations (Pt–O–M–N4) in this study.249,312–314 For other metal atomic configurations, crystal field theory with different geometries and molecular orbital theory may be employed to clarify the hybridization between eg orbitals of metal centers and O2p orbitals, and beyond. For oxygen electrocatalysis, readers are suggested to access several excellent review articles to have a detailed study of the activity descriptors in MN4 metal complexes for the ORR.246,315,316 The metal single atoms immobilized on non-carbon-based supports and the activity descriptors for the OER need to be further studied.

4.3.4.2. Transition metal-based heterostructures for the HER/HOR and OER/ORR. 
Heterostructure materials have attracted increasing research attention because of their fascinating physicochemical properties based on the hybridization of two or more components.25,318–323 By incorporating distinct compositions, the heterostructures can be altered to achieve balanced adsorption of reactant intermediates, as well as acquiring decent HOR/HER and OER/ORR activity.321,323 Specifically, the combination of two or more components normally generates an interface accompanied by a local electrostatic potential perturbation, where the strength of the interaction between components is closely associated with the type of perturbation. The generated heteroatom bonds, the induced strain effects, and the derived electronic interactions across the interface are the prominent aspects for optimization of the electronic structures of heterostructure materials as well as their enhanced adsorption capability toward reaction intermediates, leading to improved electrocatalytic reaction rates. Therefore, in this section, we concentrate on the recent progress on a variety of metal-based heterostructures encompassing carbon-based, non-carbon-based, and metal-based components that are active towards alkaline hydrogen and oxygen electrolyses, with a particular emphasis on the electronic modulation approaches as well as interpretations of the mechanism. As mentioned earlier, carbonaceous materials usually serve as catalyst supports owing to their large specific surface area, high electronic conductivity, strong stability in acidic/alkaline media, ready availability, etc.324–326 Immobilizing metals, metal oxides, metal sulfides, etc. on carbon-based supports is a widely employed strategy, because the stability is enhanced and the spatial distribution can be controlled.327 Meanwhile, the high electronic conductivity of carbon promotes the charge transfer kinetics in the heterostructure materials. Moreover, the generated interface would give rise to interesting metal-support interactions, which might exert profound effects on the electronic structure of the catalysts as well as the adsorption of the reactant intermediates.328,329 One important study that illustrates how the Ni–carbon heterointerface contributes to the enhanced HOR activity compared to pure Ni metal was reported by Yan's group in 2016.330 In this work, the Ni particles (∼20 nm) were immobilized on N-doped carbon nanotubes (Ni/N-CNT) with a loading content of around 70 wt%. Ni particles supported on CNTs (Ni/CNT) and pure Ni particles were also synthesized for comparison. The N-CNT support exerts geometrical effects that tend to decrease the strong hydrogen binding energy sites with Ni, while, on the other hand, the electronic interaction between the edge N atoms and Ni causes a further upshift of the d-band center of Ni and activates their adjacent sites for the HOR, which contribute synergistically to the improved HOR activity. Following this concept, a large number of unique metal–carbon heterointerfaces were tailored and generated by strain engineering, geometrical engineering, and incorporation of heteroatoms in the carbon skeleton (e.g., B, P, S, etc.), which contribute to improved hydrogen electrocatalysis.331–348 The success in modulating metal–carbon heterointerfaces also inspired researchers to explore the support effects coming from non-carbon-based substrates, with transition metal dichalcogenides,349–352 hexagonal boron nitride (h-BN),353–356 MXenes,357–359 cerium(IV) oxide (CeO2),360–363 and titanium(IV) oxide (TiO2),364–366 as classical representatives. Lu and co-authors synthesized a unique LixNiO/Ni heterostructure via a Li metal-mediated redox reaction, shown in Fig. 23a.367 In the O 1s XPS spectra (Fig. 23b), a dramatic ∼2.0 eV positive shift is observed for LixNiO/Ni as compared with NiO or annealed NiO/Ni, implying that the incorporation of Li+ contributes to totally different O compositions. Likewise, the Ni 2p XPS spectra also exhibit a positive shift (∼2.0 eV), and the binding energy of Ni2+ changes to 855.9 eV, which is closely associated with the Ni defects and the unique O compositions. Moreover, the O 1s XANES spectra of LixNiO/Ni compared with NiO or NiO/Ni present a pronounced adsorption peak located at 534 eV, which can be ascribed to the strong Brønsted basic oxygen. Meanwhile, a dramatic pH change (from 6.60 to 8.67) is observed on dispersing 10 mg LixNiO/Ni into 3.0 mL water, in contrast to dispersing NiO/G and Ni/G, where G stands for graphene, demonstrating that the presence of the strong Brønsted basic oxygen species could promote the dissociation of H–OH bonds. As shown in Fig. 23c, LixNiO/Ni exhibits enhanced HER activity in alkaline media (pH = 13.8) compared with that in a neutral electrolyte (pH = 7.0) because of the significantly accelerated water dissociation step due to the presence of strong Brønsted basic oxygen species. Meanwhile, the HOR performance of LixNiO/Ni in 1 M KOH is also better than for most of the reported electrocatalysts, again demonstrating the capability of the unique LixNiO/Ni interface in boosting hydrogen electrocatalysis. Apart from the LixNiO/Ni interface, CeO2 was also employed to build Ni/CeO2 heterostructures by Yang and co-workers.368 In this work, a CeO2-decorated Ni hybrid with different Ni ratios was immobilized on carbon (XC-72) to obtain CeO2(r)–Ni/C. When used as an HOR catalyst, the CeO2(r)–Ni/C-1 (52.3% Ni content) exhibited the highest kinetic current density (12.28 mA mgNi−1) at 50 mV as well as the highest ECSA normalized exchange current density (0.038 mA cmNi−2). The role of abundant oxygen vacancies in the CeO2 support in the HOR was illustrated by XPS, Raman spectroscopy, and DFT-based calculations. In Fig. 23d, the decreased hydrogen adsorption energy for CeO2(r)–Ni (−0.065 eV) as compared with pristine Ni (−0.302 eV) validates the tremendous hydrogen adsorption on CeO2(r)–Ni sites after optimization. Moreover, an increased OH* adsorption was also obtained for CeO2(r)–Ni, as its adsorption energy for OH* is –0.924 eV, in contrast to –0.163 eV for pristine Ni. The electronic interaction between CeO2(r) and Ni modulates the electronic structure of Ni, endowing CeO2(r)–Ni with improved H* and OH* adsorption capability as well as enhanced HOR performance. Very recently, Stamenkovic et al. studied the HOR performance of Pt–RuO2 heterojunctions from the viewpoint of RDE and membrane electrode assembly (MEA) investigations.369 The Pt–RuO2 heterostructures were synthesized by annealing carbon-supported PtRu8 nanodendrites, and the scaled-up synthesis (1 g per batch) was successful. The HAADF-STEM and energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) images proved that the Pt clusters and atoms were uniformly distributed in RuO2. The HOR performance of the Pt–RuO2 heterostructures was evaluated in NaOH solution and benzyl trimethylammonium hydroxide (BTMAOH). When the electrolyte changed from 0.1 M NaOH to 0.1 M BTMAOH, there was a reduced HOR current on the Pt/C Tanaka Kikinzoku Kogyo (TKK) catalyst, while only a negligible difference in the kinetics could be found for Pt–RuO2. This demonstrates that Pt–RuO2 heterostructures are less likely to be poisoned by the phenyl groups in the BTMAOH solution compared with Pt/C, suggesting its strong practical feasibility in MEAs that contain phenyl groups. Notably, the Pt mass loading in Pt–RuO2 is only 3.5 wt% as opposed to 19.4% wt% for commercial Pt/C, signifying the excellent HOR performance of Pt–RuO2 heterostructures. Furthermore, the HOR performance tested under MEA conditions is compared in Fig. 23e. The peak power density of the MEAs when using the Pt–RuO2 anode catalyst reached 0.77 W cm−2, far beyond 0.28 and 0.55 W cm−2 for Pt/C and PtRu/C, respectively. Furthermore, the peak power density as a function of catalyst cost is illustrated in Fig. 23f, showing that the Pt–RuO2 heterostructure is a promising approach to meet the U.S. Department of Energy (DOE) fuel cell cost and performance target. Some other interesting studies
on the elucidation of heterointerface effects in hydrogen electrocatalysis, including for Ni/Ni3N, Ru@RuO2/TiO2, Pt/Ni3N–Mo2C, etc., have been presented in detail, and the remarkable role of heterointerfaces in improving bonding configuration and species adsorption is demonstrated.370–372 In addition to hydrogen electrocatalysis, heterostructure engineering also plays an unavoidable role in oxygen electrocatalysis.202,354,373–381 Given the multiple reaction steps and sluggish reaction kinetics for oxygen electrocatalysis compared with hydrogen electrocatalysis, a classical catalyst design concept is to immobilize noble metals on support materials, which not only induces improved catalyst activity through strong electronic interaction, tensile or compressive strain, and confinement effects, but also results in enhanced structural stability. Based on this, Sun's group investigated the electronic effects of employing nitrogen-doped carbon, Ni(OH)2, LiCoO2, h-BN, and g-C3N4 as supports to immobilize a range of noble metals (e.g., Pt, Ir, Ru, etc.) and how the electronic effects affect oxygen evolution and reduction activity.320,354,382–384 It is interesting to find that, although Pt is not considered to exhibit oxygen evolution activity, Pt could promote the reduction of Co ions as well as the formation of O vacancies and under-coordinated Co centers, which results in weakened adsorption toward OH*/OOH* and contributes to higher oxygen evolution.320 On the other hand, h-BN and g-C3N4 both exhibit exceptional co-catalytic activity for Pd and Ir in the oxygen reduction and evolution reactions, respectively.354,382 h-BN not only increases the hydrophobicity of h-BN/Pd owing to its super hydrophobicity, which promotes gas transfer kinetics, but also induces downshifting of the d-band center of Pd due to strong compressive strain effects, thereby leading to weakened adsorption of O* as well as enhanced reaction kinetics. In-depth studies of h-BN and g-C3N4 as catalyst supports in building heterostructures have revealed their unique role in providing B, N, and C coordination environments, supplying surface wettability, and offering overall structural stability, which will yield a solid foundation for the design and construction of other heterointerfaces with wider applications. Unlike the role of h-BN in confining Pd, Dong and co-worker designed Pd/Mo2C interfaces by depositing β-Mo2C nanoparticles (NPs) into atomic layers of Pd (AL-Pd/Mo2C).385 As illustrated in Fig. 24a, the positively shifted Pd 3d peak manifests the strong interaction between Pd and Mo2C. Furthermore, the projected density of states (PDOS) of bulk Pd and AL-Pd/Mo2C shows a downshifted d-band center away from the Fermi level for AL-Pd/Mo2C. The strong electronic interaction of the Pd/Mo2C interface would result in weakened adsorption toward oxygen intermediates and faster ORR activity. Consequently, as shown in Fig. 24b and c, the half-wave potential of AL-Pd/Mo2C is 0.935 V, far better than that of most of the reported ORR electrocatalysts as well as commercial 20% Pt/C. In addition, disk and ring currents were measured, and the electron transfer number is calculated to be 3.9, suggesting that AL-Pd/Mo2C follows the four-electron reaction pathway to reduce O2 and that the H2O2 yield is negligible. Inspired by considerable work that employed noble metal species as active components in heterostructures, an increasing number of research studies are aimed at exploring the unique interface structures arising from non-noble metal species for oxygen electrocatalysis.386–388 To this end, Park et al. reported heterostructures comprising La0.5Sr0.5CoO3−δ and MoSe2 that induced a local phase transition from 2H MoSe2 to the 1T phase, thereby achieving exceptional stability of over 1000 h of operation at a current density of 100 mA cm−2.389 As shown in Fig. 24d, the electron transfer from Co to Mo is found to alter the electron configuration of Mo from the occupied 4dz2 level to the incompletely filled dxz, dyz, and dyx, to induce the phase transition as well as improve the electronic conductivity. Furthermore, the charge transfer also upshifts the d-band center of Co resulting in enhanced adsorption toward OH− as well as faster oxygen evolution kinetics (Fig. 24e). DFT calculations further confirm the electron transfer across the interface and the 0.1 eV upshift in the d-band center for Co in La0.5Sr0.5CoO3−δ. The exceptional durability of the La0.5Sr0.5CoO3−δ/MoSe2 heterostructure can be verified by the chronopotentiometric profiles in Fig. 24f, where negligible degradation in current density can be observed, even when captured at 100 mA cm−2 with an operation time of 1000 h. On the other hand, investigations of heterostructures comprising non-metal-based components in oxygen electrocatalysis have been paid particular attention to.390–392 In particular, Chen's group proposed employing carbon nanotubes (CNTs) and thienothiophene–pyrene covalent organic frameworks (COFs) to construct one-dimensional van der Waals heterostructures (1D vdWHs).392 As shown in Fig. 24g, a solution-based in situ wrapping was applied to assemble coaxial CNT/COF heterostructures, where the COF shell thickness could be controlled by changing the precursor mass ratio of COF to CNTs.
 Fig. 23  (a) High-resolution transmission electron microscope image of the LixNiO/Ni heterostructure and its reverse FFT. The S-pentacyclic domains in the thienothiophene–pyrene COF and the strong π–π interactions in the heterointerface were found to play a critical role in oxygen electrocatalysis. On the one hand, the band gap of COF/CNT vdWHs was found to decrease with the reduction of COF shell thickness, promoting the charge transfer. On the other hand, the C-S moieties were calculated to be the solely catalytic active sites for initial O2 adsorption as well as OOH* adsorption. Specifically, as shown in Fig. 24h, the O2 activation at a C atom in the vicinity of an S atom, the OOH* adsorption at an S atom, and the O* adsorption at an S atom, etc. can be illustrated with the corresponding vibration peaks of S-, as evidenced by operando FTIR. In contrast, the pristine thienothiophene–pyrene COF was characterized with a band gap of 2.08 eV and no obvious change in the vibration bands, further indicating that the efficient band-gap reduction and optimal oxygen intermediate adsorption originate from the heterointerface between the thienothiophene–pyrene COF and the CNTs. Consequently, as shown in Fig. 24i, the half-wave potential of COF/CNT vdWHs (CC-3) is 0.828 V, and the transferred electrons of CC-3 were calculated to be ∼3.86, proving its excellent ORR activity. In addition, CC-3 also delivered the lowest overpotential of 389 mV at a current density of 10 mA cm−2, much better than that of the bare TAPTt-COF (prepared by self-assemblies of thienothiophene–pyrene without CNTs) and CNTs. The development of non-noble-metal-based heterostructures not only promotes the findings of unique interface effects in oxygen electrocatalysis but also advances AEM technologies.
4.3.4.3. Transition metal alloys for HER/HOR. 
Alloy engineering normally involves modifications of the electronic structure of the parent metal through ligand effects or strain effects by incorporation of a second or even more metal phases.393 The varied electronic structures of the parent metal would give rise to a shift of the d-band center in relation to the Fermi level, inducing a changed interaction between the metal and the adsorbed molecules according to the d-band center theory.393,394 Many catalysts with enhanced HER/HOR activity and durability can be obtained by changing the electronic structure through alloy strategies.395–404 On account of the question whether oxophilic effects play different roles in alkaline HER and HOR processes, Sun's group synthesized Pt3Ni/C and acid–Pt3Ni/C to rationally compare their Had and OHad affinities, which were reflected by their CO stripping test results and cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves.405 Specifically, the Pt–OHad binding energies follow the sequence of Pt/C < acid–Pt3Ni/C < Pt3Ni/C, while the Pt–Had binding energies decrease in the order of Pt3Ni/C ≈ acid–Pt3Ni/C < Pt/C. Interestingly, the acid–Pt3Ni/C exhibits medium HER activity but delivers the best HOR activity among these three samples. The current densities at the overpotential of 50 mV (without the influence of H2 mass transfer) for Pt/C, acid–Pt3Ni/C, and Pt3Ni/C are 1.3, 6.2, and 8.3 mA cm−2, respectively, suggesting that the surface Ni species could boost the HER performance via adsorbed OHad. On the other hand, the HOR current densities (at 50 mV) for Pt/C, acid–Pt3Ni/C, and Pt3Ni/C are 1.5, 4.3, and 4.7 mA cm−2, respectively, and no apparent enhancement in the exchange current density can be observed for Pt3Ni/C as compared with acid–Pt3Ni/C, in turn demonstrating the slight effects of adsorbed OHad on the HOR. Analysis of the charge transfer kinetics based on electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) plots further confirms that the reaction mechanisms of the HER and HOR are different, which is reflected by their inequivalent transfer coefficients. For the HER process, the Ni species are used to cleave water molecules as well as to reduce the Pt–Had binding energy. With regards to the HOR, the role of Ni is to weaken the Pt–Had binding energies. The DFT calculations give hydrogen adsorption profiles as a function of the reactant intermediates in the HER/HOR process. The HER activity descriptor is reflected by the sum energy of H–OH cleaving and Had adsorption, which follows the order of Pt(111) (0.99 eV) < Pt3Ni(111)@Pt skin (0.96 eV) < Pt3Ni(111) (0.78 eV). The HOR process is merely determined by Had adsorption, where the sequence is Pt(111) (0.76 eV) < Pt3Ni(111) (0.64 eV) < Pt3Ni(111)@Pt skin (0.32 eV). This work illustrates how the oxophilic effects play different roles in the HER and HOR processes, and deepens our understanding of the HER/HOR steps although more direct evidence of the OHad behavior as a reactant intermediate is missing. In the meantime, Li's group employed in situ electrochemical surface-enhanced Raman spectroscopy (SERS) to clarify the role of hydroxyl species (OHad) in the HOR process, which might provide evidence of the dynamical reaction kinetics.406 In order to increase the surface detection sensitivity, Au metal was employed to build Au as cores with Pt or PtNi serving as shells (Au@Pt NPs vs. Au@PtNi NPs) as the main object of study. The in situ SERS spectra as a function of voltage for Au@Pt and Au@PtNi in alkaline KOH/KOD (deuterium isotopic experiment) are presented in Fig. 25a. During the HOR reaction, no Raman signals can be observed for Au@Pt, which is very likely due to the weak adsorption of OH− on the Pt surface. With regards to Au@PtNi, the peak at 778 cm−1 emerged at the potential of 65 mV and increased to a maximum intensity at 165 mV before decreasing. In terms of Au@PtNi in KOD, the peak at 778 cm−1 shifted to ∼668 cm−1, indicating that the adsorbed species are most likely to be OHad, based on analysis of the mass conversion formula.407 Moreover, DFT-based calculations were adopted to find the adsorption energy of OHad on different sites.
 Fig. 25  (a) In situ SERS spectra of Au@Pt and Au@PtNi surfaces in 0.1 M KOH, and Au@PtNi surface in 0.1 M DOH during the HOR process. (b) Illustration of the Volmer steps on pure Pt and Pt3Ni(110) surface. Reproduced with permission from ref. 406. Copyright 2021 John Wiley and Sons. (c) HER/HOR polarization curves and (d) PDOS profile for alloyed samples. Reproduced with permission from ref. 408. Copyright 2020 American Chemical Society. (e) The HOR polarization curves of Ru/C, Ni/C, Pt/C, PtRu/C, and Ru7Ni3/C, and (f) their fuel cell performance. Reproduced with permission from ref. 409. Copyright 2020 Springer Nature. (g) UPS spectra of MoNi4, WNi4, and Ni. (h) Schematic illustration of Had and OHad adsorption on Mo(W)Ni4 alloys and a comparison of kinetics currents. Reproduced with permission from ref. 410. Copyright 2020 Springer Nature. 
Of them, the adsorption of OHad on surface Ni–Ni bridge sites was calculated to feature the lowest adsorption energy. Furthermore, the co-adsorbed configuration of OHad was simulated to give the O–H rocking mode at a frequency of 774 cm−1, which matches well with the experimentally observed peak at 778 cm−1, suggesting that OHad might be another HOR intermediate species. The Gibbs free energies of Had and OHad species were also obtained by DFT calculations to clarify the HOR process. As described in Fig. 25b, for the pure Pt surface, the HOR process is energetically unfavorable due to the unavailability of sites for OHad. For the PtNi surface, however, the oxophilic Ni facilitates the adsorption of OHad and enhances the reaction rate. Moreover, the authors also inferred from electrochemical SERS that the Pt–H binding energy is identical for Au@Pt and Au@PtNi, in turn confirming the definite role of OHad in the HOR process. This is the first report that elucidates the dynamical electrochemical process for the HOR by using in–situ techniques, although the explicit role of OHad in the alkaline HER process remains to be determined. Wang and co-authors discussed the alloying effects of Ru with different metals (Co, Ni, and Fe) and demonstrated their activity enhancement mechanisms for the HER/HOR and the OER/ORR.408 With regards to hydrogen electrolysis, the specific activities and mass activities of Ru0.95Co0.05/C, Ru0.95Fe0.05/C, and Ru0.95Ni0.05/C are much higher in contrast to Ru/C and Pt/C, with Ru0.95Co0.05/C being the best (Fig. 25c). As illustrated in Fig. 25d, the d-band center of RuCo decreases with the increasing ratio of Co in the alloy, leading to weakened Had adsorption as well as enhanced HER/HOR activity.411 The recent investigations of metal-based alloys have further promoted the development of high-performance electrocatalysts in AEMWEs and AEMFCs.409,410,412 Zhuang and co-authors fabricated the RuNi alloy, and the mass activity of Ru7Ni3/C was 21 times that of Pt/C.409 Remarkably, Ru7Ni3/C exhibited a high peak power density of 2.03 W cm−2 in an H2/O2 hydroxide exchange membrane fuel cell at 95 °C, outperforming the PtRu/C anode catalyst (Fig. 25e and f). According to the refined HBE theory, the authors ascribed the highly enhanced HOR activity to the optimized hydrogen binding energy and increased water adsorption. The Ru–H binding is weakened after the addition of Ni, which was reflected by the negatively-shifted electrochemical in situ attenuated total reflection surface-enhanced infrared absorption spectroscopy (ATR–SEIRAS).413 Furthermore, the O–H stretching vibration peak derived from ATR–SEIRAS within the HOR potential range (−0.15 to 0.2 V) validates the improved water adsorption ability on Ru7Ni3/C, which is closely associated with the surface oxidized Ni species.414 Very recently, Yu's group fabricated Ni-based alloys and discussed the role of hydrogen and hydroxyl species in activity enhancement.410 As determined by extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) analysis, heteroatom bonds (Mo–Ni) are generated within the MoNi4 alloy. The incorporation of Mo modulates the electronic band structure of the MoNi4 alloy, which is reflected in the ultraviolet photoelectron spectroscopy (UPS) spectra (Fig. 25g). Compared with pristine Ni metal, the d-band center of WNi4 and MoNi4 is downshifted away from the Fermi level, implying that the metal–H antibonding state above the Fermi level is increased, resulting in weakened Had adsorption.415 Apart from the electronic effects induced by the metal alloy, the oxophilic effects are also discussed, based on the CO stripping test. The CO stripping peaks of WNi4 and MoNi4 are negatively shifted in contrast to pristine Ni metal and Pt/C, manifesting the strengthened OHad binding energy. In Fig. 25h, DFT calculations also illustrate the remarkably enhanced hydroxyl adsorption and the greatly decreased hydrogen adsorption on MoNi4, which both contributed to the boosted HOR activity. Importantly, MoNi4 exhibited a geometrical current density j0 of 3.41 mA cm−2 and an ECSA normalized j0 of 0.065 mA cm−2, which is far beyond the performance of reported non-PGM based catalysts.
4.3.4.4. Transition metal-based oxides/(oxy)hydroxides for the OER/ORR. 
Metal oxides/(oxy)hydroxides have been considered promising electrocatalysts for the OER because of their robust structural stability, strong corrosion resistance, and decent intrinsic activity. The most promising category, NiFe oxides (i.e., NiFeOxHy), has long been employed as a family of benchmark OER catalysts given their exceptional activity and economic competitiveness, especially in contrast to noble metal oxides (e.g., RuO2, IrO2, etc.).416 Although the activity of NiFe oxides is well established, the nature of active sites and their working principles remain debatable. Meanwhile, the recent catalyst design approaches involving metal oxides for the alkaline OER concentrate on foreign-metal doping and binary or ternary metal oxide/(oxy)hydroxide construction, and numerous classical catalysts have been reported with very high activity.417–419 To this end, the major objective for researchers is to elucidate the fundamental mechanism behind the OER process on metal oxides/(oxy)hydroxides, find out the catalyst design principle, and further improve the catalysts’ performance toward the alkaline OER. It has to be noted that the OER is a dynamical process with applied potential and that complex compositions, morphological changes, surface reconstruction, etc. intensify the difficulty in probing the authentic active sites. Inspired by this, Chueh and co-authors employed operando scanning probe and X-ray microscopy techniques to establish the structure–activity correlation in the oxygen evolution reaction by selecting a single-crystalline β-Co(OH)2 platelet.420 Based on combining an electrochemical quartz crystal microbalance (EQCM) with electrochemical atomic force microscopy (EC-AFM), the height and mass difference of a single β-Co(OH)2 platelet could be determined as a function of applied potentials  and are displayed in Fig. 26a. The particle height increased approximately 10% between the two applied potentials ( and ) and contracted back to its initial dimensions at . Meanwhile, the mass of the single β-Co(OH)2 platelet gradually decreased with  and . According to the EQCM measurements, the structural changes associated with applied potential are shown in Fig. 26b, where the first redox transition (0.9 to 1.36 V) corresponds to hydroxide intercalation (from Co(OH)2 to α-CoO2H1.6·0.5H2O) and the second redox reaction at  implies the removal of interlayer water and protons, and the formation of β-CoOOH. Furthermore, the author employed operando scanning transmission X-ray microscopy (STXM) to probe the Co oxidation state in the potential range of the OER (Fig. 26c), and the X-ray absorbance spectrum (XAS) with the corresponding Co oxidation state fitting is shown in Fig. 26d. The number of electrons transferred in the redox reaction was derived to be ∼0.54 ± 0.04 e− at  (∼1.14 V) and ∼0.46 ± 0.04 e− at  (∼1.58 V), respectively, in agreement with the redox peaks (Fig. 26c) and EC-AFM and EQCM results (Fig. 26a). Hence, the average oxidation states of Co were determined to be Co2.54+ at  and Co3+ at . Based on the analysis of operando STXM data and microkinetic modeling,422 the Co3+ (β-CoOOH) sites are very likely to be the active sites of the rate-determining step for the OER. This work provides a fundamental viewpoint to establish the relationship between the OER rate-determining step (RDS) and the electronic structure of a single crystal by using advanced techniques (EC-AFM and EQCM). In addition to this work, Markovic et al. aimed to provide a catalyst design scheme from the viewpoint of activity–stability and address the metal dissolution issues over the OER potential range.421 By systematically comparing the OER activity between MOxHy (M = Fe, Co, Ni) and Fe-containing MOxHy clusters (Fe–MOxHy), the activity trend toward alkaline OER (Fe–NiOxHy > Fe–CoOxHy > FeOxHy) was derived, where the OER activity was found to correlate with the surface Fe average adsorption free energy on MOxHy sites (ΔGFe–M), and the stronger interaction of MOxHy with Fe usually leads to sufficient active sites as well as enhanced stability. The OER activity enhancement for Fe incorporated 3d transition-metal hydr(oxy)oxides is shown in Fig. 26e, in which Co, Ni, and Cu could significantly improve the Fe adsorption energy owing to weakened M–O bonding. Furthermore, doping on the Ni hydr(oxy)oxide with other 3d transition metals (Co, Ni, and Cu) was confirmed to improve the Fe adsorption energy and Fe coverage, consequently leading to better OER activity (Fig. 26e). This trend was further demonstrated by the linear relationship between OER activity and Fe surface coverage on NiM (Cu, Co, Mn) and Ni hydr(oxy)oxides. At the same time, the synergistic effects between Fe and Ni species for Fe-doped NiOOH ((NixFey)OOH) in the oxygen evolution reaction have been elucidated due to their exceptional catalytic activity. Goddard and co-authors adopted quantum mechanics (QM) methods (hybrid DFT and grand canonical-QM) to find that the surface high spin d4 Fe(IV) site contributes to the formation of active O radical species; meanwhile, the surface closed-shell d6 Ni(IV) sites serve as active centers for O–O coupling. These two synergistic steps result in a highly improved alkaline OER rate for (NixFey)OOH, which is in agreement with the experimental results.423 Following this concept, the author screened the OER activity of Fe-doped NiOOH ((NixFey)OOH) by replacing Fe with 17 other transition metals and predicted Co-, Rh-, and Ir-doped NiOOH to be efficient OER electrocatalysts because they promote the formation of the key intermediate (O radical species).424 Aside from theoretical investigations, Cao's group developed a universal strategy through ligand-induced conversion on 3d-transition-metal (hydro)oxides and successfully synthesized a series of metal oxides and metal LDHs with highly improved OER performances (e.g., NiCo LDH-terephthalic acid (TPA), 267 mV overpotential).425 Given the strong bonding between *OOH species and 3d block metals, this strategy was intended to weaken the intermediate bonding by shifting the d-band center of 3d block metals, which was achieved by organic ligand transformation (e.g., replacing a certain amount of OH− by terephthalic acid). Accordingly, the downshifted d orbitals of Co and the decreased Co 3d–O 2p orbital energy gap were validated by DOS calculations and consequently led to optimized *OOH bonding and improved OER activity. On the other hand, Liu's group developed a ball milling method to introduce tensile strength into the NiFe hydroxide lattice to tune the anti-bonding orbital filling and oxygenated intermediate adsorption, as well as OER activity.426 Apart from the fabrication of transition metal hydr(oxy)oxides at the laboratory scale, Yu et al. for the first time proposed a scalable method to synthesize amorphous ternary metal oxides and studied their catalytic properties towards the OER.417 Based on the facile supersaturated co-precipitation method, the authors employed metal-containing precursors and synthesized a range of ternary metal oxides up to 515 grams with high quality (e.g., CoFeMo, MnFeMo, and CuFeMo oxides). As demonstrated, the amorphous catalysts underwent a self-reconstruction process to generate metal oxy(hydroxides) with O-vacancies during the OER, in which the O-vacancies were confirmed to lower the adsorption energy toward *OOH, consequently leading to excellent OER activity. Recently reported excellent studies particularly focused on 3d block transition metal hydr(oxy)oxides, as
can be seen from the following excellent reviews.207,427–429 Please note that surface reconstruction for metal hydr(oxy)oxides and many other metal compounds was found to heavily affect the catalyst structure and the authentic catalytic active sites especially for the OER. The studies on the surface reconstruction during the OER and the coping strategies have been systematically outlined by several review papers.207,430–432 Notably, Gao et al. emphasized the fundamental understanding of the mechanism of the surface reconstruction and summarized the strategies to tailor the reconstruction (e.g., doping, morphology tuning, vacancy, etc.) and characterization techniques for transition metal-based OER catalysts.430

(b) Illustration of the expansion and contraction of individual CoO2 layers with increasing voltage. (c) Voltage-dependent particle-averaged Co LIII-edge STXM-XAS spectra. (d) The Co oxidation state as a function of voltage. (e) Cyclic voltammogram (in 0.1 M KOH) of β-Co(OH)2 in an STXM electrochemical flow cell at a scan rate of 10 mV s−1 and a flow rate of 30 μl min−1. Reproduced with permission from ref. 420. 4.4. Gas diffusion layers and bipolar plates for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells
Gas diffusion layers (GDLs) and bipolar plates (BPs) are another two critical components to build low-cost high-performance AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. As illustrated in Fig. 7a, the GDL is usually composed of a microporous layer (MPL) and a substrate, and is in direct contact with the inner catalyst layer and the outer BP. Meanwhile, the membrane, catalyst layer, and GDL are sandwiched by BPs, and the BPs act as a shield and provide strong mechanical support to the single cell. In addition, BPs serve as protective layers to allow the chemical reaction to take place smoothly while transporting water, gas, heat, electricity, etc. A schematic illustration of a commercially available bipolar plate and the zoomed GDL structure is presented in Fig. 27a. It is believed that the properties of GDL and BP in AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells lead to very similar requirements to those of their PEM counterparts.433–436 Herein, the primary requirement on BPs in AEM devices is also to offer higher electrical and thermal conductivity to reduce the potential loss and internal heating, high mechanical strength to withstand the pressure in the cell stack, robust anti-corrosion capability to prolong the working lifetime, lower gas permeability to minimize the gas crossover, etc.433,437 In addition to this, the material density and the cost are another two factors that influence the overall function of the AEM devices. For instance, the density of the material for PEM BPs is suggested to be lower than 5 g cm−3, and the materials plus fabrication cost is believed to be lower than $0.0045 cm−2 to improve their portability and economic competitiveness.438,439 One major difference for the BPs in AEM and PEM water electrolyzers is that the BPs in AEM usually face fewer corrosion issues due to the working electrolyte; hence, the materials of the BPs and the coating layer in AEM device have a wider choice and lower production cost. For GDLs, their main function is to allow the reactants (e.g., water, gas, etc.) to pass through to the catalyst layer and, equally, let the products flow away efficiently. On top of this, higher electrical conductivity, mechanical strength, and anti-corrosion capability are desirable to improve the cell potential loss, structural stability of the membrane electrode assembly, and cell durability. The properties of the BP and GDL for AEM devices are displayed in Fig. 27b, and we next discuss how to make rational material designs for BPs and GDLs based on the requirements mentioned. There have been increasing research reports on BPs for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells,440,441 but there has been very little in-depth exploration on how the employment of various materials and/or flow channel designs determine the cell performance. In the case of the BPs in PEM fuel cells, the selection of different raw materials, ranging from graphite and metals (e.g., Al, Ti, Ni, etc.) to composites (e.g., stainless steel, carbon composites) has been comparatively studied.439 Furthermore, a variety of modification strategies, including changing the filler content and incorporation of protective coating layers to improve the conductivity and anti-corrosion capability of the BPs, have been widely adopted,433,442–444 which in turn indicates that relevant studies on the AEM devices are urgently needed. Moreover, the different operation conditions between AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells also call for comprehensive studies on the BPs to respectively develop ideal ones for the two devices. A typical example is that the BPs of AEM water electrolyzer anodes tend to suffer from severe corrosion in comparison to their counterparts in AEM fuel cells because the potential experienced at the anode can be often higher than 2.0 V. As shown in Fig. 27c, three respective flow channel designs, i.e., 1-channel serpentine, 5-channel serpentine, and parallel, are depicted. The flow channel transition from 1-channel serpentine to multipath serpentine reduces the flow resistivity and favors a high flow rate (Fig. 27c).445 Continuing this concept, parallel channels and modifications were accordingly proposed to reduce the hydraulic resistivity.446,447 A schematic illustration of a flow field channel from a PEM BP is shown in Fig. 27d to provide fundamental insight on how to achieve efficient BPs in AEM devices through the rational design of channel parameters. Therefore, from the viewpoint of experimental design and computational modeling, finding optimal channel parameters such as channel depth, flank angle, pitch distance, etc. for AEM devices is urgently needed. In addition to the basic channel parameters, a common problem of BPs is the increased interfacial contact resistance (ICR), which originates from the generated metal oxidation layer at the surface of BPs after a certain period of operation. In particular, the anodic film formation is rather severe because the potential is usually higher than +1.5 V vs. RHE. Targeting this problem, Proch et al. studied a series of coating materials used in the BPs for AEM fuel cells and explored how the coating layer helps to reduce interfacial contact resistance.448 The ICR was calculated through an established setup, and the BP surface compositions were detected by glow-discharge optical emission spectroscopy (GD-OES), the results of which are presented in Fig. 27e. Optical images of the surfaces of BPs and the ICR were found to show no difference before and after exposure to aqueous sodium hydroxide with pH 11 and pH 14.5. Furthermore, the GD-OES profile tested under the conditions of pH 11 showed that C and O signals were mostly dominant, which is almost the same as with the pristine one. When the pH of the electrolyte was changed to 14.5, the GD-OES profile exhibited an increased O signal, indicating obvious carbon oxidation. Interestingly, the ICR remained almost unchanged, even with severe carbon oxidation, suggesting that the carbon coating could well inhibit the generation of metal oxides. This work paves the way for the future design and development of robust BPs for AEM fuel cells and water electrolyzers. Next, we consider three classical experimental factors (i.e., pore structure, hydrophobic/hydrophilic conditions, and thickness) for which a GDL will be evaluated to study its performance in AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells, respectively. For a macroporous layer (MPL), Razmjooei and co-authors proposed to use a Ni-based MPL to further improve the performance of AEM water electrolyzers.450 Specifically, the Ni-based MPLs were synthesized by using the air-plasma spraying (APS) technique, in which a very porous Ni-based alloy layer with the thickness of ∼80 μm was deposited on a metallic porous transport layer (PTL), thus forming a Ni-based MPL. As shown in Fig. 28a, the total pore volume of the NiMPL-PTL was 11.29 mm3 g−1, and the pore size ranged from 0.1 to 90 μm, which are far better than those of an uncoated PTL (total volume of 0.85 mm3 g−1 with pore size ranging from 0.01 to 0.1 nm). The highly improved porous structure of NiMPL-PTL promoted gas and water transportation, leading to enhanced cell performance (Fig. 28b). The AEM water electrolyzer with NiMPL-PTL precious metal catalysts (Ir and Pt/C) delivered the lowest voltage (1.9 V) at a current density of 500 mA cm−2, which is much lower than that of the cell (2.19 V) employing a normal PTL and identical catalysts as well as of the cell using a commercial Ni mesh as the PTL. On the other hand, NiMPL-PTL also enables the reduction of interfacial contact resistance (ICR), which is another reason for the improved cell performance. Nyquist plots of cells with PTL/PTL and NiMPL-PTL/NiMPL-PTL, captured at the current density of 500 mA cm−2, are presented in Fig. 28b. The internal and mass transfer resistances of the NiMPL-PTL/NiMPL-PTL cell are both far lower than for the PTL/PTL cell, again manifesting that NiMPL-PTL could efficiently reduce the ICR and promote gas removal. In addition, the complicated water management for AEM fuel cells in comparison with their PEM counterparts makes the tuning of the hydrophobic conditions of the GDL rather significant and challenging. For this purpose, Yang et al. investigated the effects of the hydrophobic conditions of the gas diffusion layer on the AEM fuel cell performance through modulation of the amount of polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE) within the GDL.451 In this work, two gas-diffusion substrates (GDSs: carbon paper functionalized with/without PTFE) and three gas-diffusion layers (GDLs, GDSs further coated with a MPL) were prepared and characterized accordingly. The pore size distributions (PSD) of the five samples are given in Fig. 28c, where GDS-0 shows a maximum distribution of the pore diameters at 33 μm when compared with GDS-30 and GDL-30. In addition, the intensity of the maximum distribution for GDL-30
decreases in comparison with GDS-30, indicating that the incorporation of MPL has occupied the pores on GDS and hence leads to reduced maximum distribution intensity. Furthermore, the porosity (%), air permeability, through-plane resistance, and mean pore diameter for GDS-0, GDS-30, GDL-0, GDL-10, and GDL-30 were calculated. The air permeability of GDL-0, GDL-10, and GDL-30 gradually increases while the through-plane resistance of the former two remains nearly identical (5.45 vs. 5.43 mΩ cm−2), and the latter is increased to 11.9 mΩ cm−2, suggesting that a thicker PTFE layer in the GDS results in better gas transfer capability but also causes higher resistance. The polarization curves and power density profiles of the cells assembled with different GDLs are presented in Fig. 28d. Specifically, the cell voltage and power density profiles of the cells show the trend of GDL-0/GDL-0 < GDL-40/GDL-40 < GDL-10/GDL-10 < GDL-30/GDL-30. This indicates that too high a PTFE content in the GDL does not necessarily lead to higher cell performance and that 30% PTFE functionalization on the GDL benefits water transport more than 10% PTFE does, which contributes significantly to cell performance. In addition to the two strategies, tuning the thickness of the GDL is another method to improve cell performance, because the thickness of the GDL would greatly affect the electrical conduction, the passage of reactants/products, and the framework of the cell. As illustrated in Fig. 28e, Zhang and co-authors employed a novel liquid/gas diffusion layer (LGDL) with much-reduced thickness and tunable pore morphologies.452 Compared with the conventional titanium foam GDL (e.g., mesh, foam, felts, etc.) with a thickness of ∼260 μm, the bare thin/tunable liquid/gas diffusion layers (TTLGDLs) feature a thickness of approximately 50 μm. Meanwhile, a ∼80 nm-thick gold layer was electroplated on the surface of TTLGDL (surface-coated TTLGDL (SC-TTLGDL)). The average pore size of TTLGDL was found to be 300 μm, which is far better than the commercial Ti foam GDL (∼30 μm). Consequently, an AEM water electrolyzer with the SC-TTLGDL delivered much enhanced current density compared with an SC-Ti foam-based cell at the same overpotential, whether operated under 0.1 M KOH electrolyte or deionized water (Fig. 28f), suggesting that the highly porous and thinner LGDL promotes mass diffusion and interfacial contact. Moreover, the decreased thickness of LGDL also helps to build a cell stack with reduced weight, product cost, and volume. On this basis, more in-depth studies on the GDL from the perspectives of pore size, hydrophobic conditions, and thickness are still needed to find the ideal elements for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells.
 Fig. 27  (a) Architectural view and images of the bipolar plate (BP) and gas-diffusion layer (GDL). (b) Key properties of the BP and GDL, and the normal tuning strategies from a variety of aspects. (c) Illustration of different types of flow channels in the BPs in PEMFCs. Reproduced with permission from ref. 449. (d) Cross-sectional view of the flow channel and the critical geometrical parameters. Reproduced with permission from ref. 439. Copyright 2020 Elsevier. (e) Optical images of stainless-steel grade 304L BPs of AEMFCs exposed to three electrolyte conditions and the GD-OES profiles. Reproduced with permission from ref. 448. Copyright 2020 Elsevier. 
 Fig. 28  (a) 3D structure of NiMPL-PTL and its pore size distribution profile.
