4.5. Membrane electrode assembly (MEA)
The membrane, ionomer, electrocatalyst, gas diffusion layer, and bipolar plate have been carefully summarized, and now we move on to the assembly of these key fragments to understand the fabrication process for a single cell and cell stacks. A key step is the construction of the membrane electrode assembly (MEA), which is usually manufactured by two different pathways: catalyst-coated substrate (CCS) and catalyst-coated membrane (CCM).94,453 In the catalyst-coated substrate (CCS) the catalyst ink needs to be dispersed on the gas-diffusion layer to form a gas-diffusion electrode (GDE).454 MEA is then generated by hot-pressing a single layer of AEM sandwiched between two GDEs.455 In sharp contrast, the catalyst-coated membrane (CCM) is fabricated by hot-pressing a single layer of AEM sandwiched between two catalyst layers (CLs).94 Generally, the CCM-based MEA possesses a higher catalyst utilization efficiency and electrochemical reaction rate than the CCS-based MEA because the reaction that occurs on the carbon paper for the CCS-based MEA is a more lengthy process.93,456 Meanwhile, the studies of CCM-based MEAs in PEM fuel cells demonstrated that this type of MEA could efficiently minimize noble metal loading, improve cell power density, and increase overall performance.93,456,457 The employment of CCM-based MEAs would also benefit the catalyst loading and cell performance, which has also been validated in several studies on AEM fuel cells.457,458 On the other hand, the process of fabricating CCM is rather complicated, and obtaining high-quality MEAs is quite challenging because of the partial dissolution of the membrane with the catalyst ink solvent, especially for membrane thickness lower than 20 μm. In addition, membrane creep behavior was found to be present in CCM and reduce the durability of the membrane.459 Critically, the comparisons between CCS-based MEAs and CCM-based MEAs under the premise of employing non-noble-metal-based electrocatalysts still need to be studied to further clarify the most suitable MEA fabrication methods and the compatible non-noble-metal-based catalysts. Here, the manufacturing approach for the single cell and cell stacks based on the CCM-based MEA is presented in Fig. 29. After the fabrication of the CCM and the gas-diffusion layer (GDL), a 5-layer MEA is formed by hot-pressing CCM sandwiched between two layers of GDL. Gaskets and seals are usually assembled with the 5-layer MEA to avoid any gas and/or liquid leakage. Finally, a 7-layer MEA is generated for further assembly with the bipolar plate and end plate before obtaining a single cell as well as cell stacks.
 Fig. 29  Manufacturing flow process for membrane electrode assembly and cell stacks for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. (a) Catalyst coated membrane, (b) gas-diffusion layer, (c) a single cell, and (d) cell stacks for AEMWEs and AEMFCs. 
4.6. Electrochemical evaluation and state-of-the-art performance of AEM devices

4.6.1. Electrochemical evaluation. 
Electrochemical evaluation is the first and foremost step to determine if the cell components have been properly assembled and how well the cell is operated. In the past few decades, tremendous studies have concentrated on catalyst development and optimization, and most of the performance has been assessed in the three-electrode configuration (TEC) (Fig. 30a). The TEC can give a quick and unified measurement of the as-prepared catalysts, but it cannot reflect the precise working conditions in the MEA system (e.g., interfacial structure, reactant concentration, local and bulk pH, etc.).460,461 What is worse is that some electrocatalysts reported with superior performance in TEC do not necessarily deliver equivalent performance when assessed in MEA-based cells,462 further implying that a realistic measurement method to simulate the true working environment is a great necessity. In view of this, Kim et al. employed a microelectrode (ME) set-up to study the HOR and ORR reaction kinetics at the Pt/ionomer interface, and found that cell preconditioning benefits the HOR reaction kinetics of Pt in tetramethyl guanidinium (TMG) and benzyl trimethyl ammonium hydroxide (BTMAOH).463 Weber et al. proposed the pros and cons when using ME to investigate the solid-state electrolyte.464,465 Although a ME has competitive advantages when studying lots of variables (e.g., humidity, temperature, gas flow, etc.) in solid electrolyte membranes than TEC, it is rather complicated to screen cell components than MEA does. For the MEA system, the electrocatalyst and membrane have both achieved outstanding progress, and the current challenge is how to translate the developed high-performance catalysts from liquid electrolytes to the membrane-electrode assembly (MEA).81,466 Therefore, the catalyst evaluation method from TEC to MEA and to a practical water electrolysis cell/H2–O2 fuel cell can be a necessary transition, which could boost the optimization of a range of cell components and operational conditions.453 On this basis, the electrochemical evaluation based on a single cell and the testing advantages as well as the protocols are illustrated in Fig. 30b–d. Fig. 30b shows a MEA-based water electrolysis cell with a working electrode area of 1 cm2. Therefore, the as-prepared catalyst, membrane, ionomer, etc. can be evaluated under these MEA conditions. Currently, this type of single cell can be built at a laboratory and/or directly purchased.467 The electrocatalysts (e.g., 20% Pt/C, IrO2, etc.) as well as membranes (e.g., FAA-3-10 and X37-5) with reproducibility and reliability are commercially available.103 These premises make the establishment of a unified testing protocol for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells a high possibility.441,467 For instance, it is suggested that, for each test, it is better to select the other cell components except for the object of research (catalyst or membrane) from commercial benchmarks, in which a relatively reliable and reproducible polarization curve of a specific research object can be obtained for accurate evaluation. In Fig. 30c, the use of the MEA mimics the practical working environment (water electrolyzer stacks) for every component, which establishes a reliable and unified method to screen cell components and working parameters.462,468
 Fig. 30  Translation of electrochemical evaluation methodology. (a) Three-electrode configuration (TEC), (b) Components and structure of a single cell with working electrode 1 cm2 in size. (c) Advantages and necessity of a single cell configuration evaluation in comparison with TEC. (d) Proposed single cell test protocol. 
Based on the analysis of the literature, the testing protocol for an MEA-based single cell is illustrated in Fig. 30d.


4.6.2. State-of-the-art AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. 
AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells have gained solid progress very recently, owing to the technological advances on electrocatalysts, membranes, ionomers, etc. On the other hand, the efforts toward the optimization of operational conditions and the cell configuration also contribute significantly to the high performance of AEM devices. Herein, AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells with state-of-the-art performances and a variety of cell tunings from the aspects of the operational conditions and engineering setup are presented. First, some typical durability curves of AEMWEs and their corresponding testing protocols are illustrated in Fig. 31a and b. For an AEMWE tested under a constant current density, the polarization voltage curve usually expresses the following different behaviors: type 1 to type 4, which correspond to a gradual increase in voltage, gradual decrease in voltage, stepwise increase in voltage, and spike type of voltage. The gradual increase in voltage is the most common type of degradation that appears in an AEMWE, which might be caused by the increase in cell resistance arising from the loss of cationic functional groups of the AEM. The opposite type 2 degradation is speculated to be due to AEM thinning, leading to hydrogen crossover, although the voltage profile does not reflect the true degradation process. The stepwise increase in voltage is associated with the loss of fast catalyst activity or a local pH change that result in rapid performance decay. Lastly, spike type 4 represents a recoverable degradation within a certain amount of working time, which is thought to be due to changes in the liquid electrolyte concentration or water transport issues. For the detailed durability curves and the possible assumptions, readers are suggested to refer Li's work.469 Compared with the intensive studies on the degradation of PEM devices and the durability tests,470,471 investigations of the AEM water electrolyzers are still in an early stage. As shown in Fig. 31b, Lim's group studied the effects of two different durability testing protocols (short periodic and long periodic modes) on the stability of AEM water electrolyzers.472 For protocol A, the cell is subjected to a bias of 1.95 V for 2–10 hours and rested for 12 hours at open circuit without solution feeding. As a comparison, the 1.95 V voltage bias was prolonged to ∼100 h after every 12 hour rest for protocol B. The AEM water electrolysis cells are operated under the two protocols for 1000 h; meanwhile, the impedance spectra of the cell at a bias of 1.8 V are obtained. Based on the analysis of the ohmic and non-ohmic resistances of the cell, it was found that the frequently rest processes led to an increase in the ohmic resistance. Furthermore, more rest times also led to a lower humidity level inside the cell and degraded the membrane. These findings might provide useful tips on the operation of practical AEM water electrolyzers, in that it is a necessity to feed the electrolyzer to protect the membrane from deterioration, even under regular rest. Sung and co-authors carefully investigated the possible factors influencing the performance of AEM water electrolyzers (Fig. 31c and d).473 The authors found that the CCM-based cells delivered a higher current density than the CCS-based cells with and without hot-pressing. In addition, the resistance analysis made it clear that the CCM-based cell showed minimum ohmic, charge transfer, and mass transfer resistance. In contrast, the CCS-based cell with hot-pressing showed similar ohmic resistance (∼0.2 Ω) but with higher charge transfer resistance, indicating that the CCM fabrication method could promote the electrochemical reaction kinetics. Furthermore, the CCS-based cell without hot pressing exhibited a much higher ohmic resistance (∼0.7 Ω) and rather higher charge transfer as well as mass transfer resistance. The evidence confirms that CCM is more suitable for use in the fabrication of MEAs. As shown in Fig. 31d, the cell fed with the 1 M KOH electrolyte at both the anode and cathode showed the best performance, while the cell fed with the 1 M KOH electrolyte at the anode and pure water at the cathode delivered much reduced current density at the same overpotential. The use of pure water as a reactant at the anode and at both electrodes led to unstable cell performance, manifesting that the supply of KOH at the anode and cathode results in improved performance. Following this concept, a range of careful tunings on the catalysts, membranes, MEA fabrication method, operation conditions, etc. can be accessed from lots of interesting works.476–479 In addition to the operation conditions and electrocatalyst, the durability of AEM water electrolyzers and the test protocol warrant further investigations.472,480–482Fig. 31e and f give two typical examples showing that record-high current density and durability have been achieved for AEM water electrolysis cells.474,475 In light of the consensus that the critical challenge for membranes is durability and mechanical stability, Meroueh selected Sustainion® X37-50 as the membrane and experimentally reinforced the membrane by the sol–gel method to validate its long-term durability.474 The cell using PTFE reinforced Sustainion® (Grade-T) membrane exhibited a record-high durability (Fig. 31e) with only ∼8 mV degradation within 12000 h of operation. The exceptional durability this reinforced membrane achieved demonstrates that an industrial-level lifespan for an AEMWE can be achieved through a rational membrane reinforcement strategy. According to the different requirements on ionomers in anodes/cathodes, Lee and co-authors employed poly(fluorenyl-co-biphenyl piperidinium) (PFBP) with a high IEC (3.43 mmol g−1) and high water uptake (∼350%) as cathode ionomer and adopted poly(fluorenyl-co-terphenyl piperidinium) (PFTP) bearing a high IEC (2.80 mmol g−1) and low water uptake (∼80%) as the anode ionomer, in which the water molecules from the AEM can be efficiently captured at the cathode.475 In addition, poly(fluorenyl-co-terphenyl piperidinium) (PFTP) was selected as the anion-exchange membrane.475 In particular, ionomers and membranes with a range of fluorenyl ratios were tested in the AEMWEs, from which the ideal fluorenyl ratios for electrode ionomer and membrane were acquired. As shown in Fig. 31f, a record-high current density of 7.68 A cm−2 at 2.0 V for an AEMWE was achieved by feeding 1 M KOH as the electrolyte, surpassing that of the state-of-the-art PEMWE (6 A cm−2 at 2.0 V). The current density of AEMWEs with the use of PGM-free electrocatalysts still reached a high level (1.62 A cm−2 at 2.0 V), suggesting the strong competitiveness and practicability of AEMWEs compared to the PEMWE.
 Fig. 31  (a) Typical voltage degradation curves as a function of operation time for AEMWEs. (b) The proposed long-term durability test protocols for AEMWEs. Reproduced with permission from ref. 472. Copyright 2021 Elsevier. (c) AEMWE performance comparison as a function of the MEA fabrication method (CCM vs. CCS) and the resistance analysis. (d) AEMWE performance comparison as a function of the working electrolyte and the feeding flow rate. Reproduced with permission from ref. 473. Copyright 2019 Elsevier. (e) Record-high long-term durability of AEMWEs with a range of AEMs. Reproduced with permission from ref. 474. Copyright 2021 Elsevier. (f) Polarization curves of AEMWEs with specific cell configurations to achieve record-high current density, and the comparison of current density and durability between PFAP membrane and those reported membranes. Reproduced with permission from ref. 475. Copyright 2021 RSC Publishing. 
State-of-the-art performances of AEMFCs with the employment of typical anode/cathode catalysts are depicted in Fig. 32. For AEMFCs, long-term cell durability (>1000 h) has rarely been reported; hence, understanding the cell degradation mechanism and whether or not the performance losses can be recovered by cell optimization is rather significant. To this end, Mustain et al. continued their previous study and investigated the cell durability mechanism and performance loss reversibility.196,483 As claimed in the previous work, the authors employed benchmark electrocatalysts (PtRu/C for anode and Pt/C for cathode), ionomers, and GDEs, and achieved a stable operation of 2000 h.196 Within this period of 2000 h, the cell average degradation rate nearly maintains at 15 μV h−1 (Fig. 32a). However, an apparent rise in high-frequency resistance (HFR) from 6 to 9 mΩ was observed in the time frame of 1050 h to 2000 h (∼3 μΩ h−1). The rising rate of HFR can be translated to a voltage degradation rate of 9.5 μV h−1, implying that the increased HFR took a majority of the voltage loss within this period. Between 2000 and 2800 h, the voltage degradation rate sharply increased to 118 μV h−1 accompanied by the rise of HFR (4.6 μΩ h−1). Notably, the HFR-induced voltage loss simply accounts for 13.8 μV h−1, suggesting that many other factors are involved in the degradation of cell performance. The authors hence ascribed the performance losses to two types of degradation. The first one is reversible degradation that is very likely induced by the cathode dry-off and water imbalance. This means that the reversible degradation can be recovered by cell optimization such as changing the electrode Dew point. Another part is an irreversible loss that is mainly caused by cell component degradation (e.g., catalysts, ionomer, membrane, etc.). To verify this assumption, the authors increased the Dew point of the cathode to improve the water conditions. At around 2630 h, the Dew point at the anode and cathode both increased obviously, the cell voltage slightly recovered, and the degradation rate reduced (Fig. 32a and b). The authors concluded that nearly half of the lost cell performance can be recovered in the period from 2000 to 2600 h by resolving the water balance issue. The cell with the optimized cell parameters also exhibited better PPD than those without operation optimization (Fig. 32c). This work demonstrates that attaining a longer and better cell durability is very promising by frequently cell optimization, which also opens an avenue to develop durable and robust AEM fuel cells in the future. Fig. 32d–i give the polarization curves and PPD for several AEM fuel cells with the employment of anode, cathode, and membrane. In Fig. 32d–f, three representative cathode electrocatalysts, i.e., Fe–N–C, Mn–Co spinel oxides, and Co@C/C, have been assembled with benchmark anode catalyst (PtRu/C) to test the cell performance.43,107,484 The AEM fuel cell with the use of Fe–N–C as the cathode electrocatalyst exhibited an excellent PPD of 1.44 W cm−2 and a remarkable mass transport limited current density of 4.3 A cm−2. To the best of our knowledge, this is the highest PPD of the reported AEM fuel cells with the employment of a non-precious metal cathode. Zhuang and co-authors prepared [Mn0.3Co0.7][Mn0.6Co0.4]2O4 (MCO) as the cathode and the assembled AEM fuel cell delivered a PPD of 1.1 W cm−2 and a higher mass transport limited current density of 3.5 A cm−2.484 In Fig. 32f, Chen focused on the development of poly(fluorene-co-biphenyl N,N′-dimethylpiperidinium) (PFBP) as ionomer and poly(fluorene-co-terphenyl N,N′-dimethylpiperidinium) (PFTP) as the membrane. The PFTP-based fuel cell with the use of cobalt catalyst (0.6 mg cm−2 Co@C/C, 40 wt% Co) delivered a PPD of 0.891 W cm−2.107 On the other hand, some typical precious metal-free anodes and the fuel cell performances are presented in Fig. 32g–i. Atanassov et al. synthesized Ni95Cu5 nanoalloy that immobilized on Ketjenblack as the anode material for an AEMFC.485 With the use of Pd/XC-72 as the cathode, the AEM fuel cell exhibited a PPD of ∼0.35 W cm−2 at 80 °C. Zhuang prepared Ni-based nanoparticles enwrapped by a 2 nm nitrogen-doped carbon shell (Ni@CNx) as the anode and employed Pt/C as the cathode (Fig. 32h). Specifically, the assembled AEM fuel cell exhibited a PPD of 0.48 W cm−2 at 80 °C. Meanwhile, the fuel cell presented quite stable durability for 20 h at a current density of 600 mA cm−2. Notably, changing the cathode catalyst from commercial Pt/C to [Mn0.3Co0.7][Mn0.6Co0.4]2O4 (MCO), the AEM fuel cell achieved a record high PPD of 0.21 W cm−2 (Fig. 32i). One should note, these works have slightly different catalyst loading amounts although most the electrode catalysts have selected benchmark materials, manifesting a unified comparison with identical cell components and working conditions is rather important and critical. The recent AEM water electrolysis and fuel cell performances are displayed in Tables 4 and 5.

Reproduced with permission from ref. 483. Copyright 2022 American Chemical Society. 5. Working management for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells
5.1. Feeding electrolyte
Three different types of commonly employed feeding electrolytes for AEMWEs and their polarization curves have been briefly introduced in Fig. 5e. Simply speaking, the use of different types of electrolytes and electrolyte pre-feeding methods need careful tuning to achieve an optimal balance between cell performance and long-term durability.70,72,73,492–495 First, Park's group confirmed that adequate membrane wetness to ensure sufficient OH− doping in the membrane is required to decrease the membrane ohmic resistance and complete the membrane activation, which is significant for the initial operation of AEMWEs.492,493 Hence, they suggested that pre-feeding 0.5 M aqueous KOH into an AEM water electrolysis cell could maximize the cell performance. Razmjooei et al. found that an improved cell performance is achieved by changing the electrolyte solution from 0.1 M KOH to 0.3 M, although this performance increase is rather limited when the electrolyte solution further changes from 0.3 M KOH to 1.0 M KOH.496 This is because a minor increase in electrolyte concentration contributes to accelerated HER and OER kinetics, reduced ohmic resistance of the membrane, and fast ion mobility. Further increasing the electrolyte concentration (above 0.3 M KOH) leads to increased solution viscosity and blockage of catalytic active sites due to the formation of gas bubbles on the surface. As shown in Fig. 33a, Mustain and co-authors studied the cell performance and durability through tuning a series of KOH solutions with different concentrations. They found that diluted KOH solution benefits cell performance but very likely leads to reduced long-term durability compared with the use of DI water.495 The exceptionally low cell degradation for over 500 h (93.5 μV h−1) when feeding with DI water vs. higher cell performance when feeding with diluted KOH solutions (Fig. 33a) manifests that the delicate balance between durability and cell performance needs careful studies. On the other hand, Weber and co-authors elucidated the fundamental role of metal alkali solution in AEMWEs through experiments and mathematical models.494 As shown in Fig. 33b, the voltage loss of the AEM water electrolysis cells with three different electrolytes can be grouped into anode kinetics-intrinsic, anode kinetics-pH, anode kinetics-bubble, cathode kinetics, high-frequency resistance (HFR), catalyst layer ohmic resistance, and ion-exchange resistance by using a mathematic model. For the cell fed with DI water, the most dominant factors contributing to the voltage loss are anode kinetics-intrinsic, catalyst layer ohmic resistance, anode kinetics-pH, and HFR resistance. In terms of the cell using a 0.01 M KOH solution, these four factors still dominate the voltage loss but all with reduced proportions. By contrast, for the cell fed with 1 M KOH solution, anode kinetics-intrinsic was also regarded the most influential factor of the voltage loss while the others took up minor shares. This is a pioneering study where a mathematic model analysis method was employed to quantitatively compare all possible factors involved in cell voltage loss. Therefore, a more precise improvement strategy can be proposed to address the challenges such as increasing the intrinsic activity of the anode catalyst, facilitating membrane and ionomer transportation, and reducing the HFR. Besides, Weber's group also proposed to thoroughly investigate the effects of the ion type (cationic and anion), the cation/OH− ratio, and the presence of carbonates on the cell voltage and durability.497,498 As depicted in Fig. 33c–e, three important aspects, i.e., cationic ion type, anion ion type, and different cation/OH− ratios in determining the cell voltage and durability are presented. The employment of 1 M KOH, 1 M NaOH, and 1 M LiOH as electrolytes aimed to validate whether the AEM water electrolysis cell performance followed the trend of KOH > NaOH > LiOH as observed from the RDE configuration (half-cell). In this work, the hydration-free enthalpy and exchange time of a water molecule in a hydration shell for cations were determined, where Li+ appears to have a higher hydration-free enthalpy and water exchange time than those of Na+ and K+. Consequently, the author speculated that LiOH is very likely to precipitate close to the anode layer of the membrane and hence block the water supply. This is why the polarization curves (kinetics region (0 to 50 mA cm−2)) of the AEM electrolysis cells fed with 1 M KOH deliver improved voltage profiles compared to cells fed with 1 M LiOH, 1.45 M LiOH (equivalent conductivity with 1 M KOH), and 1.23 M NaOH (equivalent conductivity with 1 M KOH). For current density above 2000 mA cm−2, the precipitate further inhibits the catalytic active sites and causes an increased voltage. In terms of the better performance for the cell fed with LiOH in comparison to KOH in the ohmic region (250 to 1600 mA cm−2), it is assumed that the higher defect density and ionic mobility for the Li+-interfacial double layer (IDL) accelerate the mobility of OH− and result in lower overpotential. Following a similar mechanism, the cationic effect on the cell performance is concluded to exist in the order K+ > Na+ > Li+, in which an identical trend was observed in the RDE configuration. In addition, the authors employed electrolytes with identical pH values and conductivities (0.5 M KOH (13.65, 189 mS cm−1), 0.82 M K2CO3 (11.35, 189 mS cm−1), and 18 mM KOH (11.22, 7.4 mS cm−1)) to critically compare the cell performances (Fig. 33d). Notably, the cell voltage with the three electrolytes delivered the trend of 0.5 M KOH > 18 mM KOH > 0.82 M K2CO3 in the kinetics current region, while the voltage trend changed to 0.5 M KOH > 0.82 M K2CO3 > 18 mM KOH in the current density range of 700 to 2000 mA cm−2, indicating that the cell performance is not solely related to the conductivity or electrolyte pH. The authors ascribe these findings to the cation/OH− ratio, which is further discussed in Fig. 33e. In general, the increased cation/OH− ratio leads to increased overpotential. The weaker performance of the 0.82 M K2CO3-fed cell than the 18 mM KOH-fed cell was due to the higher cation/OH− ratio. These mentioned works give a detailed elucidation of the fundamental insights on the effects of using different types of electrolytes, electrolyte concentrations, ion species, etc. on the cell performance. Care needs to be taken to attain a balance between cell performance and durability, for which systematic work on the AEMWE cells to find the optimal feeding electrolyte and compatible cell components (e.g., non-noble-metal-based electrocatalyst, ionomer, GDL, etc.) is further required.
 Fig. 33  (a) Voltage degradation profiles for AEMWEs operating under three different electrolyte feeding conditions. Reproduced with permission from ref. 495 Copyright 2022 IOP Publishing. (b) Voltage breakdown analysis for AEMWE cells feeding with three respective electrolytes: DI water, 0.01 M KOH, and 1 M KOH. Reproduced with permission from ref. 494. Copyright 2021 IOP Publishing. Polarization curves of AEMWEs influenced by the types of (c) cations, (d) anions, and (e) cation/OH ratios. Reproduced with permission from ref. 497. Copyright 2021 IOP Publishing. 
5.2. Water and gas management
As shown in Fig. 34a, water is solely generated in the cathode area (occurs via the ORR reaction) for PEM fuel cells. For AEM fuel cells, water serves as both the reactant and the product at the cathode and anode, respectively, implying that water shuttling between electrodes in AEMFCs is rather complicated than for their PEM counterparts.499,500 For every 4 moles of electrons that pass through the circuit, two moles of water molecules are consumed at the cathode, while 4 moles of water molecules are generated. In addition, accompanied by the movement of hydroxide ions from the cathode to the anode, a certain amount of water molecules is dragged by the OH− and transported through the membrane to the anode, which is also referred to as the electroosmotic drag. On the other hand, back diffusion, in which water molecules come across the membrane driven by the water concentration gradient between the electrodes also occurs. Importantly, Fig. 34b illustrates the significance of water balance within the AEM fuel cells from two critical aspects: the membrane and the catalyst layer. Too high a water content usually causes water flooding, and conversely, too low a water content leads to dry-off, both exerting badly hazardous effects on the cell components. Taking the membrane as a typical example, a higher amount of water usually increases the water uptake of the membrane, which would reduce its mechanical stability and durability. Too low a water content makes the membrane brittle and deactivates the ion transportation within the membrane. In terms of the catalyst, water flooding very likely blocks the catalytic active sites and is the main reason for the loss of catalytic activity. Furthermore, water flooding also causes improper transport of gas/water/heat within the MEA, which would lead to fast degradation and cell failure. The effects of imbalance of water management on the cell components would dictate the cell performance and durability. Consequently, suggestions from the viewpoint of cell components and operation conditions on how to make rational modulations to further improve water management in AEMFCs have been presented (Fig. 34b). As shown in Fig. 34c, Mustain's group has coupled neutron imaging and operando electrochemical measurements to give a clear summary of the water distribution within the cell components.501 It was found that with increasing operation temperature, a higher amount of liquid water accumulates in both the anode catalyst layer and the GDL, while almost no water content variation can be observed at the cathode CL and GDL. Furthermore, a linear increase in the thickness of the liquid water at the anodic GDL is observed as the working temperature changes from the dew point to the optimum +2 °C (Fig. 34d). Notably, an increase in liquid water content is accompanied by a reduction in the cell voltage, certifying that an increase in relative humidity (RH) contributes to increased cell voltage. It has to be noted that the authors employed two membranes (radiation-grafted ETFE-BTMA vs. perfluorinated AEM) with different water uptake capabilities to validate this concept. Without any doubt, both membrane-based cells exhibited almost an identical water accumulation trend in the anodic catalyst layer and GDL, further indicating the universality of this finding. Based on these findings, the authors proposed to improve the water capacity of the anode to optimize cell performance through manipulation of the anode catalyst content ratio between the ionomer, carbon, and Pt. As a result, with an increased carbon ratio in the anode catalyst layer and in the overall thickness, the capability of the anode to withstand water improved significantly, which can be evidenced by the greatly enhanced mass transport limiting current (5 A cm−2) and peak current density (1.9 W cm−2). Following this concept, Mustain further employed operando neutron imaging and X-ray computed tomography (CT) to illustrate the liquid water distribution under various operating conditions.502 For an AEM fuel cell operated at full humidity, the neutron image manifests that a large number of water molecules have accumulated in the membrane and electrode. In contrast, the accumulation of water content is significantly alleviated with a decrease in the dew point of reactant gases. In particular, the cell water distribution dependence on the operating current density and dew point is further illustrated in Fig. 34e. The AEM cell with a higher working current density is found to have a higher amount of liquid water in every part of the cell (anode/cathode GDL, anode/cathode CL, and membrane) compared with the cell operated at a low current density. This proves that, at a higher current density, water is very likely to be dragged from the anode to the cathode via back diffusion, indicating that the current density serves as a main driver for cell water accumulation. Based on this observation, the authors proposed to redesign the electrode with the employment of an optimal amount of hydrophobic PTFE to balance the water production rate while maintaining high RH of the gases. These works contributed by Mustain's group give us fundamental insight into the importance of the water balance in AEM fuel cells and how the cell architecture should be rationally designed to properly cope with this challenge. Apart from above-discussed works, many studies from the aspects of the membrane, ionomer, GDL, catalyst, etc. on making careful modulation of the water content in AEMFCs have been presented.503–508 The electrode parameters (e.g., GDL, ionomer content, catalyst loading amount, and the ratio of active material to carbon) and cell operation conditions (e.g., flow rate and gas dew point) are found to heavily affect the membrane hydration and electrode flooding/drying out, for which a delicate balance between all the parameters is needed to develop robust AEMFCs.499,504,507
 Fig. 34  (a) Comparison of the water distribution in PEMFCs and in AEMFCs. Reproduced with permission from ref. 499. Copyright 2018 Elsevier. (b) The common problems of water management in AEMFCs from the viewpoint of membranes and catalysts, and the tuning strategies for water management from the standpoint of cell components and operating conditions. (c) Operando in-plane neutron radiographic images of water in an ETFE-BTMA AEM-based AEMFC (anode/cathode: 50 °C/50 °C) vs. perfluorinated AEM (PFAEM)-based AEMFC (anode/cathode: 54 °C/51 °C). (d) Water distribution plots from the data in (c). Reproduced with permission from ref. 501. Copyright 2018 RSC Publishing. (e) Operando in-plane high-resolution neutron radiographic images of water distribution in ETFE-BTMA-based AEMFCs operated under four conditions. Reproduced with permission from ref. 502. Copyright 2020 Springer Nature. 
As shown in Fig. 35a, Jang et al. employed the solvent-assisted molding (SAM) technique to functionalize membranes with dual-sided patterns to improve the capability in water management.507 Here, a commercial membrane FAA-3–50 (FuMA-Tech) was selected, in which a dual patterned hydrocarbon-based AEM was fabricated. As depicted in Fig. 35b, the modified membrane exhibits enhanced ionic conductivity and improved mass transfer of O2 and H2 gas, which contribute to higher AEMFC performance. In addition to the membrane, Yoo and co-workers proposed to manipulate water management from the standpoint of the catalyst material by improving its capability in water transfer via incorporating a thermo-responsive polymer.505 Specifically, poly-(N-isopropylacrylamide) (PNIPAM), showing the characteristic of hydrophobicity at the operating temperature, was employed to functionalize the electrocatalyst to enhance water management. The normal carbon black was modified with PNIPAM (with the product denoted as CTRP), and was then used as a support to load Pt to obtain Pt/CTRP. In Fig. 35c, the water distribution within the anode and cathode for four different catalysts (Pt/C, Pt/CTRP, Pt/C + C, and Pt/CTRP + C) is presented, where a balanced water content at the anode and cathode appears for the Pt/CTRP + C electrode, implying that PNIPAM does benefit the water back diffusion from the anode to the cathode. In addition, the enhanced hydrophobicity arising from the employment of PNIPAM is reflected by the increased water contact angle, as described in Fig. 35c. Consequently, Pt/CTRP could efficiently drive the water to move from the anode to the cathode, which cannot be achieved for a normal Pt/C electrode (Fig. 35d), even with a supply of humidified air at the cathode. The AEM fuel cells with the use of the Pt/CTRP + C electrode also exhibited higher PPD and max powder density (Fig. 35e and f). This work proposes a new avenue to manipulate water balance from the aspect of the catalyst, which is interesting and needs to be further studied. On account of the relative maturity of PEM technology over its AEM counterparts, hydrogen crossover has been mostly reported in PEM fuel cells and water electrolyzers.509–511 It usually refers to the permeation of two gases (H2 and O2 for H2–O2 fuel cells and/or water electrolyzers) across the polymer membrane during operation, in which the phenomenon is exacerbated with an increasing operating pressure.512,513 The concern of hydrogen crossover also appears for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells, although the H2 pressure for the state-of-the-art AEMWEs (∼30 bar) may not be larger than for PEMWEs (30 ∼ 80 bar).33,36 This is because the H2 crossover rate is heavily dependent on a variety of factors, including membrane thickness, membrane permeability, current density, working temperature, hydrogen supersaturation, etc. Meanwhile, the distinct features of the AEM in comparison with the PEM (e.g., thickness and permeability) and different operation parameters dictate that H2 crossover in AEM devices might not follow the identical pathways as that of their PEM counterparts. For water electrolyzers, the gas crossover would decrease the purity of H2, pose safety issues, and cause a decrease in cell efficiency.511 In fuel cells, the H2 crossover also raises hazardous safety problems and generates additional heat, peroxides, and water molecules, eventually leading to reduced cell efficiency, accelerated thermal and chemical degradation, and reduced overall performance.509,510 The intrinsic factors that cause the H2 crossover of AEM devices are presented in Fig. 36a. First, the porous feature of the ion-conducting membrane implies that gas shuttling across the membrane is unavoidable. The H2 permeation caused by this factor takes up only a tiny minority. Second, the post-engineering processes including the fabrication of the CCM and MEA, the cell stacks installation, etc. usually incorporate a certain number of cracks and flaws into the membrane, which makes the hydrogen crossover easier. Third, the membrane degradation during operation makes it thinner and induces pinhole formation in the membrane, which intensifies the H2 crossover significantly. The three factors influencing H2 crossover in PEM devices have been widely documented, and the latter two are found to play a dominant role. For AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells, the above-mentioned reasons are thought to account for the H2 crossover, and an in-depth understanding of each category needs to be further provided. The H2 crossover, in turn, causes degradation of the catalyst, the membrane, and the whole cell (Fig. 36b). The gases that have crossed will combust and release an amount of heat that will induce local hot spots in the membrane and membrane thermal degradation. Meanwhile, the radicals associated with gas crossover usually attack carbon or nitrogen branch points of the membrane and hence intensify the chemical degradation. In the case of the catalyst, the generated heat and radicals also cause the sintering and agglomeration of catalyst particles as well as loss of catalytic activity. For a detailed discussion of the origin of gas crossover, the consequences, and corresponding mitigation strategies, the reader is encouraged to refer to Ming's work.514 Based on the analysis of the factors determining hydrogen crossover in AEMs, it is reasonable to conclude that there are two aspects to mitigating the crossover. Increasing the membrane thickness, reducing the defects during the fabrication processes, and alleviating the membrane degradation during practical operation are widely employed. Guiver and co-authors developed microporous quaternized ammonium modified polymers of intrinsic microporosity (PIM) (QPIM-1) AEM bearing high hydroxide conductivity, and studied how the hydration state affects the crossover.515,516 As shown in Fig. 36c, QPIM-1 membranes with three respective thicknesses (34, 53, and 74 μm) were employed to fabricate MEAs, and the hydrogen crossover current densities at 50 °C at various relative humidity (RH) values were evaluated. There was an obvious rapid increase in the current density of H2 crossover up to 0.15 V, and a following gradual increase was observed for three QPIM-1 membranes. For the three membranes, decreasing the RH from 100% to 60% resulted in a negligible change in the H2 crossover current density, but a further decrease to 40% led to a rapid increase in the H2 crossover current density, indicating that the 60% hydration state might be a threshold for water to block the microporous structures in the QPIM-1 membrane. The water sorption analysis illustrates that the microporous structure can be occupied by water molecules; hence, changing the hydration status might be an effective approach to prohibit H2 crossover in AEMFCs (Fig. 36d).
 Fig. 35  (a) The fabrication process for a dual-sided patterned membrane by the solvent-assisted molding (SAM) technique. (b) Illustration of the promotion effects in water management of the membrane. Reproduced with permission from ref. 507. Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society. (c) Change in the water content of the cell electrode, based on the improvement in water management using Pt/C, Pt/CTRP, Pt/C + C, and Pt/CTRP + C electrodes. (d) Water contact angle results for Pt/C and Pt/CTRP electrodes at 60 °C. (e) Cell polarization curves of MEAs with Pt/C, Pt/CTRP, Pt/C + C, and Pt/CTRP + C as anodes for AEMFCs. 5.3. Thermal management
For water electrolyzers and fuel cells, the additional heat mainly comes from two aspects. For one, ohmic heating mainly comes from the key components arising from the current passage through. For another, electrochemical heat (entropic heat) mainly derives from partial electrochemical energy conversion. The accumulated heat will cause a temperature gradient in the cell components and consequently, lead to fast cell degradation and failure.78,517 Therefore, a variety of cooling techniques including coolant fluid, cooling channels, heat pipes, and the employment of BPs with ultrahigh thermal conductivity are employed to mitigate the overheating problems. Due to the relatively fewer studies of heating management for AEM devices, readers are suggested to read some excellent reviews on thermal management for electrochemical devices, where several effective strategies that have been demonstrated for PEM counterparts might provide novel ideas for AEM devices.78,518,519 Therefore, the fast development of AEM technologies also urges the research community that thermal management studies in the field of AEMWEs and AEMFCs need to be focused on to help address practical technical problems.
6. Operation monitoring for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells
With the fast development of key components and operational management for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells, advanced techniques used to monitor the dynamic operation of cell devices and the key components are in high demand. Herein, a series of real-time observation technologies, including electron microscopy, spectroscopy, and X-ray spectroscopy techniques will be discussed. X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS), particularly synchrotron XAS, has played a significant role in the electrocatalyst community owing to its sensitivity and strength in deciphering atomic configurations and elemental coordination environments. The fundamentals and recent progress on XAS in electrocatalysis have been extensively documented, and readers are suggested to refer to the works reported by Duan, Feng, and Li.254,310,520 Apart from the electrocatalyst, challenges arising from many other key components and related operational problems remain to receive much attention, in which a variety of in situ and/or ex situ techniques will play a decisive role. These include in situ fluorescence spectroscopy,521operando neutron radiography,522–524 nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR),139 infrared spectroscopy,525etc. In this section, the unique techniques with important roles in visualizing the behavior of key components in operation, deciphering membrane/ionomer/MEA degradation pathway, and revealing working principles and management mechanisms will be discussed.
6.1 X-Ray computed tomography
X-Ray computed tomography (CT) is a nondestructive technique used to visualize the microstructure and internal composition of a test sample and present its three-dimensional distribution viewed from many directions.526,527 An illustration of a laboratory X-ray CT is presented in Fig. 37a, where a series of X-ray transmission images of the sample are obtained by the detector. Owing to its prevailing advantages, X-ray CT has been widely applied in water electrolyzer and fuel cell technology, including membranes,528 catalysts,529 GDLs,524 water management, etc. In particular, X-ray CT has also become rather significant in detecting and analyzing surface and interface structures such as triple-phase boundaries, in view of its high sensitivity and spatial resolution. In this section, we will particularly select some interesting works which have used X-ray CT for the characterization of membranes, catalysts, and/or GDLs in AEM water electrolyzers and/or fuel cells in an attempt to promote the fast advancement of operation monitoring techniques in AEM technology. Xu and co-workers targeted the membrane-electrode interfacial compatibility and proposed to fabricate a tightly bounded interface between the AEM and the electrode.491 The VBBQPPO-based MEA was in situ converted into crosslink-immobilized by a gradual thermal treatment under N2 atmosphere. Consequently, a dense 3D-zipped interface layer (3D-ZIL) and a tight chemical contact were formed for the MEA (denoted as ZIL-MEA) compared with normal MEA without thermal treatment. Micro-X-ray tomography was employed to investigate the interface structure of the ZIL-MEA. In Fig. 37b–d, the crevices and cracks can be observed at the interface for normal MEAs, while no cracks can be detected for 3D-ZIL, demonstrating the tight interface connection. In addition, X-ray CT was also used to detect the catalyst layer and examine water diffusion and the catalyst failure mechanism.530 Based on the advantages of employing non-noble-metal-based materials as electrocatalysts for AEMFCs, Zenyuk's group fabricated Fe–N–C as an electrocatalyst and employed ex situ nano-CT and operando micro-CT to visualize water distribution within the catalyst layer. As shown in Fig. 37e–h, a micro-X-ray CT, together with two nano X-ray CTs of the cathode catalyst layer at 50% and 100% RH, respectively, is presented. Apart from the MEA and the catalyst layer, X-ray CT can be also used to justify the water transport conditions by detecting the water conditions in the gas-diffusion layer. To this end, Zenyuk et al. selected four types of commercially available gas diffusion electrodes, namely, the Freudenberg, Toray, SGL 10BA, and SGL 24BA, and employed X-ray CT to study their water transport behavior.531 One should note that previous reports normally interpreted the external contact angles (CAs) of GDLs as their internal wettability due to the lack of an efficient method to accurately test the internal properties. In this work, the authors adopted an algorithm method and computed the local CAs from the pore space images that were captured with the use of X-ray CT.531,532
 Fig. 37  (a) Illustration of a laboratory X-ray CT instrument that is composed an of X-ray source, sample stage, and detector. Reproduced with permission from ref. 526. Copyright 2021 Springer Nature. X-ray CT images of (b) ZIL-MEA, (c) M-MEA-B, and (d) M-MEA. Reproduced with permission from ref. 491. Copyright 2021 John Wiley and Sons. (e) Cross-sectional micro-X-ray CT image of a catalyst layer (CL) and (f) the zoomed images at 50% RH and 100% RH. (g–h) Three-dimensional representations of the CL. Reproduced with permission from ref. 530. Copyright 2018 Elsevier. (i and j) 3D maps of contact angles (CAs) and local roughness of four different types of GDLs. Reproduced with permission from ref. 531 Copyright 2021 American Chemical Society. 
The 3D maps of the CAs and the local roughness of the four GDLs (from left to right SGL 10BA, SGL 24BA, Toray H-060, and Freudenberg H23C2) are shown in Fig. 37i and j. In addition, the probability distributions of the CAs for the four GDLs were simulated, where a mixed wettability can be observed for all GDLs. The mean internal contact angle values for the GDLs were calculated following the sequence: Freudenberg (96°) < Toray (98°) < SGL 10BA (102°) < SGL 24BA (112°). All the contact angle values were further used to calculate the wettability percentage of the GDLs, and it was found that SGL 24BA was the most hydrophobic, while Freudenberg was the most hydrophilic, in accordance with the mean internal CA values. It was concluded that the hydrophilicity is attributable to the surface metal oxide groups, while the hydrophobicity can be correlated with the PTFE content and roughness to trap air. This work also highlights the importance of using X-ray CT in deciphering water transport in GDLs, which will lay a solid foundation for the investigation of other components and operational issues by using X-ray CT.524,533
6.2. 
          In situ spectroscopy and electron microscopy testing
Aside from X-ray CT, in situ spectroscopy and electron microscopy techniques, including Raman spectroscopy, Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR), and transmission electron microscopy (TEM), have been widely reported in articles on membrane technologies and beyond.525 Vibrational spectroscopy techniques such as Raman spectroscopy plays an increasingly important role in characterizing O–H vibration bands and the interfacial water structure at catalyst/electrolyte interfaces, which has provided valuable insight for deciphering ion/species adsorption and the catalyst design rationale.406,534,535 TEM and scanning TEM (STEM) enable the observation of the dependence of sample morphology and atomic structure evolution on the applied potential. The fundamental principles of these techniques have been presented in several excellent review articles;536,537 hence, we focus on the employment of these techniques in the field of AEM technology to summarize how the techniques benefit the development of catalysts, membranes, ionomers, etc. The studies on the employment of various operando spectroscopic techniques in AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells have been discussed previously.205,406
 Fig. 38  Pathways toward practical deployment of AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells from fundamentals to technologies. 
7. Conclusions and perspective
Water electrolyzers and H2 fuel cells are regarded as ideal clean energy solutions, which could efficiently utilize renewable resources and secure a sustainable society. To fulfill this vision, developing low-cost high-performance key components, including anion-exchange membranes, ionomers, electrocatalysts, bipolar plates, gas-diffusion layers, etc., determining the optimal operation conditions, including the electrolyte, working temperature, water/heat management, etc., and employing advanced operando techniques (e.g., XAS, X-ray CT, Raman spectroscopy, etc.) to provide feedback for the device design are critical and unavoidable (Fig. 38). The fast advancement of catalysts and anion-exchange membranes, to some degree, have propelled the development of AEM technologies, although state-of-the-art advances in some key aspects, namely, membrane durability, working management, and operation monitoring are far from adequate. A fundamental understanding of the membrane failure mechanism, water transfer dynamics, and catalyst activity descriptors remain to be provided with new insights, not even mentioning more comprehensive and challenging aspects, such as MEA fabrication, water management, etc. Therefore, we present following the perspective on what needs to be further studied from a range of aspects to accelerate AEM technology development and translation into applications:
(1) From the viewpoint of key components, a low-cost high-performance membrane featuring long durability is the key target for AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. This is because membranes essentially determine the viability and practicability of these two devices. Critically speaking, current studies on the AEM focus on ionic conductivity and mechanical stability; however, the durability, especially the long-term durability under practical working conditions (10000 h or longer), is rarely reported. It has to be noted that the obtained membrane durability tested under either ex situ conditions or in mere KOH solutions cannot accurately reflect the authentic working environment in AEM water electrolyzers and/or fuel cells. Consequently, membrane electrode assembly (MEA) based cells and/or cell stacks are suggested to be used for the assessment. Furthermore, standard testing protocols (Fig. 30d) are required to give reliable and repeatable evaluation and screening for membrane materials. In addition, the catalyst layers, comprising the electrode electrocatalysts and ionomers, are the second biggest challenge to overcome. This is because the triple-phase boundary is an exceptionally critical environment that requires efficient transport of gas, liquid, and electrons, while it is easily influenced by many conditions, including the water content, temperature, layer thickness, ionomer/catalyst ratio, gas stream flow, etc. Moreover, the slightly different requirements between anode and cathode imply that the catalyst layer design for the anode and the cathode should be different, suggesting a viewpoint from the whole device (water electrolyzer and/or fuel cell) to carefully evaluate and regulate their respective anode and cathode layer parameters is a high necessity. Given that the catalysts and ionomers are used together to fabricate the catalyst layer, a strong binding property of ionomer/catalyst is another important aspect that needs to be further developed to improve the overall durability. Apart from the aforementioned aspects, improvements in the gas diffusion layer and bipolar plate to increase the mass transfer capability, cell efficiency, and cost-effectiveness, are necessary. The use of MEA-based cells to evaluate and screen catalysts and materials is very important, as the requirements on a single component might be incompatible.
(2) From the viewpoint of operational management, optimal operation conditions to attain cell activity and durability need to be comprehensively studied and modeled. In terms of AEM water electrolyzers, the key operational conditions, including temperature, working electrolyte, and feeding flow rate, need to be balanced to achieve long-term operation. The cell performance is easily affected by the parameters of these operational conditions; hence, the operation conditions should also be optimized from a standpoint of a single cell or more cells, which would provide reliable and realistic data for practical operation. In the case of AEM fuel cells, CO2 concentration and water management are two critical parameters that affect the cell performance, in addition to the temperature, gas stream flow rate, etc. Developing membranes with high withstand capability to CO2 is one approach, which means that the use of ammonium-based membranes might not suitable because they tend to be attacked in the presence of CO2. A fundamental understanding of the membrane failure mechanism induced by CO2 is desirable, which would help in designing membranes that are less likely to be affected by CO2. Another plausible solution is gas stream management. A CO2 filter can be considered to mitigate the CO2 pollution problem from its source, even with the sacrifice of cost. Self-purging and developing CO2-resistant membranes and catalysts need to be further studied though they will also bring some other issues.538 Cell stack overheating and gas crossover are problems that commonly appear in AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. Gas crossover not only reduces the cell efficiency but also increases the safety problems because of the potential for explosion with the increasing concentrations of hydrogen and oxygen. Overheating also causes a decline in cell efficiency and intensifies cell stack degradation, leading to a significantly reduced lifespan. Consequently, cooling fans and water are normally employed to ease the overheating of the cell system. It is reasonable to conclude that the success of low-cost, high-performance cell stacks of AEMWEs and AEMFCs is impossible without careful operational management, mainly including the electrolyte, water, heating, gas, etc.
(3) Third, advanced and operando characterization techniques would provide valuable feedback for the materials design and architecture engineering in AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells. Considering the multiple solvated ions or species involved in the HER/HOR, advanced characterizations, particularly the surface-sensitive spectroscopy methods, are suggested to probe the changes in the surface and interface structure during the electrochemical reactions. Surface-enhanced Raman spectroscopy (SERS) has been adopted to validate the role of OHad species in the alkaline HOR/HER.406 Likewise, surface-enhanced infrared absorption spectroscopy (SEIRAS) has been employed to study the adsorption of cations or hydrogen on the Pt surface.223,539,540 Apart from the aforementioned advanced characterizations, the in situ techniques should also be coupled with them to monitor the real-time changes in catalyst surfaces and solid-electrolyte interfaces during the HER/HOR reactions. As for the catalysts themselves, in situ XAS is a representative tool that can provide precise information on the electronic structure and configurations for certain metals in catalysts. These techniques have already been employed to study the coordination evolution of Pt single atoms in the HER.541 Jia and co-workers employed in situ XAS to study the HER/HOR kinetics of Pt after the addition of transition metals.239,542 Following this concept, other in situ techniques such as in situ Raman spectroscopy and Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy might also be helpful to determine the dynamical changes.
(4) Last but not least, theoretical simulations or calculations are highly necessary. DFT-based calculations could provide more theory-led design concepts to guide material design strategies in the future.210 On the other hand, the theoretical calculations are also helpful for understanding the complex reaction mechanisms where multiple species are involved, which would play a unique role in deciphering the membrane failure mechanisms and catalyst dissolution dynamics. We hope that a clear understanding of every key component will lay a very solid foundation for the construction of AEMWEs and AEMFCs. In addition, by coupling the advances on operational management and operation monitoring techniques, low-cost high-performance AEM water electrolyzers and fuel cells are expected to show fast progress and deployment in the near future.

Table Content:

Peng Li
	Peng Li received his PhD degree under the supervision of Distinguished Prof. Shi Xue Dou from the Institute for Superconducting & Electronic Materials at the University of Wollongong in 2021. Currently, he is a postdoctoral researcher for a joint project “Low-cost electrolysis of seawater and CO2 for co-production of renewable fuel and hypochlorite” between RMIT and CSIRO Energy under the mentorship of ARC Future Fellow Prof. Tianyi Ma. His current research focuses on the rational development of efficient materials, and the fundamental understanding of the electrochemical processes for electrocatalysis and fuel cells.

Wenping Sun
	Wenping Sun is a professor at School of Materials Science and Engineering, Zhejiang University. He received his B.S. degree in 2008 and PhD degree in 2013 in Materials Science from the University of Science and Technology of China (USTC). He is a recipient of the ARC DECRA (2016) and CAS Outstanding Doctoral Dissertation Award (2015). His research expertise includes electrocatalysis, fuel cells, and batteries, especially the design of novel materials and structures, and fundamental understanding of the related electrochemical processes. He has authored/co-authored over 130 peer-reviewed papers, which have attracted a total citation of >12000 with an h-index of 59 (Google Scholar). He serves as an Associate Editor for Frontiers in Chemistry and Materials for Renewable and Sustainable Energy.

Shi Xue Dou
	Shi Xue Dou is a Distinguished Professor at the University of Shanghai for Science and Technology, University of Wollongong (UOW) Research Ambassador Asia and the founding director of Institute for Superconducting & Electronic Materials at UOW. He received his PhD at Dalhousie University, Canada in 1984, DSc at the University of New South Wales in 1998 and was elected as a Fellow of the Australian Academy of Technological Science and Engineering in 1994. He was awarded the Australian Government's Centenary Medal in 2003 and Australian Order of Member in 2019 for his contribution to materials science and engineering, multiple Australian Professorial Fellowships from 1993 to 2011, the Vice-Chancellors Senior Excellence Award in 2008, Outstanding Partnership Award in 2012, the ASTS Lifetime Achievement Award in 2018, Lifetime Achiever by Australian Research Magazine in 2020 and the ICMC Lifetime Achievement Award in 2021. He is named a highly cited researcher in materials science by Thomson Reuters since 2018 with citations of 90000 and an h-index of 145. He has supervised and co-supervised over 100 PhD students, and more than 60 postdoctoral and visiting fellows. He is a program leader for Auto CRC 2020 on electrification program and the on-going ARENA 2016–2021 on smart sodium storage system program.

Tianyi Ma
	Tianyi Ma is a Professor and the Leader of Carbon Neutrality Group at RMIT University. He received his PhD in physical chemistry in 2013 from Nankai University, China. He was awarded an ARC DECRA in 2015. He has published more than 230 papers which attracted more than 22000 citations with an h-index of 69. He is a world-leading scientist in renewable energy field, with pioneering work done in the areas of functional photocatalytic, electrocatalytic, thermocatalytic, and piezocatalytic materials for renewable solar, mechanical, and thermal energy harvesting storage and utilization, carbon capture, utilization and storage (CCUS), as well as next-generation high-performance battery and supercapacitor energy storage devices. He is an ARC Future Fellow, Fellow of Royal Society of Chemistry, and Clarivate's Global Highly Cited Researcher.

Hongge Pan
	Hongge Pan received his PhD in materials science and engineering from Zhejiang University in 1996 under a joint program between Zhejiang University and Institute of Physics, Chinese Academy of Science. Later that year he joined Zhejiang University and became a professor in 1999. He is a recipient of the Changjiang Distinguished Professorship by the Ministry of Education (2014) and National Outstanding Doctoral Dissertation Award (2000). His research is focused on energy materials for solid-state hydrogen storage, lithium-ion batteries, electrocatalysis, and photocatalysis. He has authored/co-authored over 380 peer-reviewed papers, which have attracted a total citation of more than 11000 times with an h-index of 55. Now he serves as a co-editor-in-chief of Journal of Alloys and Compounds.
